وصفة وغيرهما ، وقد أشعر أيضاً بالاحتياج إلى المخصص : الإتيان بالجمع في ( العالمينَ )؛ فإنَّهُ مُؤذِنٌ بالاختلاف في المقادير والصفاتِ والأزمنة والأمكنة ، معَ قبولِ كلِّ مقدار غيره وصفته وزمانه ومكانه ، فلو وقع ذلكَ مِنْ غيرِ فاعل .. لزمَ الجمع بين متنافيين ؛ وهما مساواة أحد الأمرين لصاحبه ورجحانه عليه بلا سبب، وذلك معلوم الاستحالة . فإذاً ؛ هذا الوصف ؛ وهو ( ربُّ العالمينَ ) مُؤذِنٌ بحدوثِ جميعِ العوالم مِنْ جهةِ المضافِ؛ لإشعارِهِ بعموم التربية للعوالمِ المستلزمة للتغير في جميعها ، وهو دليل على الحدوثِ والافتقار للمحدثِ ، ومِنْ جهة المضاف إليه ؛ لإشعارِهِ بسبب جمعيَّتِهِ وعمومه باختلافِ أصنافِ العوالم وأنواعها وأجناسها في مقاديرها وصفاتها وأزمنتها وأمكنتها وجهاتها ، معَ قَبولِ مادَّةِ كلَّ واحدٍ منها لِمَا حصلَ لغيره ، وذلكَ يستلزم حدوثها وافتقارها إلى المخصص . ولمَّا كانَ الإحداث والإيجاد موقوفين على كمال ألوهية الموجدِ ، واتصافه : بوجوبِ الوجودِ ، والقدم ، والبقاء ، والقيام بالنفس والمخالفة للحوادثِ ، والوحدانية ، والحياة ، وعموم القدرة والإرادة لجميع الممكنات ، وعموم العلم لجميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات .. لزم أنَّ كلَّ حادث يدلُّ على وجوب هذهِ الكمالات لمولانا جل وعلا . وبالجملة : فالعوالم بعد أن تقرَّرَ وجوب حدوثها وافتقارها إلى مولانا جلَّ وعلا .. شهدَتْ بأنَّ كلَّ كمال قديم فهو وصفه تعالى ؛ لتوقف حدوثِها على اتِّصافِ مولانا جل وعلا بذلك الكمال ، وشهدَتْ بأنَّ كلَّ كمال حادثٍ فهو فعلُهُ تعالى ؛ لِمَا شهدَتْ بِهِ مِنْ وجوبِ الوحدانية لمولانا تبارك وتعالى ؛ فقد شهدَتْ إذا بأنَّ المدح بكل كمال قديم أو حادثٍ إِنَّما هو لمولانا جل وعلا ، وهو معنى ( الحمد لله ) . وهذا التقريرُ يُعرِّفُكَ : أَنَّ تعقيب جملة ( الحمدُ للهِ ) في سورةِ ( الفاتحة ) بالوصف بـ ( ربّ العالمينَ ) . . هو هو في غاية الحُسْنِ والإعجاز ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
And description and others. And it also suggested the need for a specific agent: the use of the plural in (al-'ālamīn), for it indicates difference in measures, attributes, times, and places, with the capacity of every measure for another measure, its attribute, its time, and its place. So if that occurred without an agent, it would necessitate the combination of two mutually exclusive things: equality of one of the two things to its companion and its preponderance over it without cause, and that is known to be impossible. So then, this description, which is (Rabb al-'ālamīn), indicates the contingency of all worlds from the aspect of the added (al-mudāf), because it suggests the generality of upbringing for the worlds, which entails change in all of them, and it is a proof of contingency and need for the originator; and from the aspect of the added-to (al-mudāf ilayh), because it suggests by its being plural and its generality the difference of the kinds, types, and genera of the worlds in their measures, attributes, times, places, and aspects, with the capacity of the matter of each one for what occurred for another, and that necessitates their contingency and need for the specific agent. And since creating and bringing into existence depend on the perfection of the divinity of the creator and His being qualified with necessity of existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity to contingent things, unity, life, generality of power and will over all possible things, and generality of knowledge over all necessary, contingent, and impossible things, it necessarily follows that every contingent thing indicates the necessity of these perfections for our Master, mighty and majestic. And in summary: the worlds, after it has been established that their contingency and need for our Master, mighty and majestic, are necessary, testified that every eternal perfection is His attribute, because their contingency depends on our Master, mighty and majestic, being qualified with that perfection; and they testified that every contingent perfection is His act, due to what they testified to of the necessity of unity for our Master, blessed and exalted. So they testified then that praise for every eternal or contingent perfection is only for our Master, mighty and majestic, and that is the meaning of (al-ḥamdu lillāh). And this exposition informs you that the placement of the phrase (al-ḥamdu lillāh) in Sūrat al-Fātiḥah followed by the description (Rabb al-'ālamīn) is extremely beautiful and miraculous, and with Allah Almighty is success.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument based on the Qur'anic phrase 'Rabb al-'ālamīn' (Lord of the worlds). The author first draws attention to the plural form 'al-'ālamīn' (the worlds) which indicates difference in their measures, attributes, times, and places, while each part could potentially have been like another. If this diversity occurred without a free agent, it would entail a contradiction: either two things would be equal yet one would be preferred without cause—which is impossible. Therefore, a specific agent (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is required. The description 'Rabb al-'ālamīn' thus indicates the contingency of all worlds in two ways: from the aspect of the added (al-mudāf), i.e., 'Rabb' (Lord), which implies upbringing and change, and from the aspect of the added-to (al-mudāf ilayh), i.e., 'al-'ālamīn', whose plural implies diversity and mutual receptivity, again necessitating a specific agent. Next, the author notes that creation and bringing into existence require the perfection of the creator's divinity, listing necessary attributes such as necessity of existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity to contingent things, unity, life, and omniscience. Thus, every contingent thing implies these perfections for Allah. In summary, after establishing the contingency of the worlds and their need for Allah, the worlds testify that every eternal perfection is an attribute of Allah (because their contingency demands that the creator be qualified with that perfection), and every contingent perfection is an act of Allah (due to His unity). Therefore, all praise belongs to Allah, which is the meaning of 'al-ḥamdu lillāh'. The author then remarks on the eloquence of placing 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' before 'Rabb al-'ālamīn' in Sūrat al-Fātiḥah, showing the harmony between meaning and expression.

Key Terms
رب العالمين
مضاف
مضاف إليه
حدوث
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'Rabb al-'ālamīn' is merely a descriptive title without logical implications.
Correction: Sanusi demonstrates that its grammatical structure and semantic content provide a rational proof for the contingency of the worlds and the existence of a creator.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the plural 'al-'ālamīn' indicate?
A) That all worlds are identical
B) That the worlds differ but each could have been otherwise
C) That the worlds are eternal
D) That there is only one world

Key Takeaway
The diversity of the worlds cannot be uncaused; the phrase 'Rabb al-'ālamīn' itself provides a logical proof for contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at the variety in your own life — your different abilities, circumstances, and changes over time. Can you truly attribute all of this to chance or your own planning? Or does the fact that things could have been otherwise force you to admit a specific Agent behind your existence? What is stopping you from acknowledging Him as Rabb today?
?
The phrase 'Rabb al-'ālamīn' is on your lips daily. Have you ever paused to consider its proof? Are you reciting it as mere praise, or does it transform your awareness of Allah's constant tarbiya and your utter dependence on Him? How would your day change if you truly believed every diversity in your life is a sign of His specific choice?
?
Sanusi argues that without a specific agent, the diversity of the worlds would entail a logical contradiction. Yet in your daily decisions, you often act as if you are the ultimate cause of your circumstances. Where have you assumed self-sufficiency, ignoring that your very existence and each of your attributes are contingent, needing a Mukhassis? What is one area you need to surrender to Allah's Lordship today?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
7 min
Divine Attributes Implied by Contingency
Sanusi deduces divine attributes from contingency and concludes praise belongs to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of the worlds necessitates that the Creator possesses a set of necessary perfections, and why all praise belongs to Him.

Core Idea
Contingent things point to the necessary perfections of their Creator.

Having established the contingency of all worlds, Sanusi now draws the theological implications. He argues that the act of creation requires the Creator to be qualified with attributes such as necessity of existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity to contingent things, unity, life, and perfect knowledge and power. He then concludes that every perfection – whether eternal (as an attribute of Allah) or contingent (as an act of Allah) – ultimately deserves praise to Allah alone, which is the meaning of 'al-ḥamdu lillāh'. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from 'ولما كان' to the end
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the list of attributes and the logical flow from contingency to divine perfection.
The passage connects creation to divine attributes.
2
Identify the list of necessary attributes
Sanusi lists: necessity of existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity to contingent things, unity, life, and omniscience/omnipotence. Understand each term.
These are standard in kalam theology.
3
Understand the logical link
The passage states that creation depends on the perfection of the Creator's divinity. Therefore, every contingent thing indicates these perfections. Why? Because the contingency demands a cause that possesses the power and will to create.
4
Grasp the conclusion about praise
The worlds testify that every eternal perfection is Allah's attribute (since contingency depends on it) and every contingent perfection is His act (since He alone is the creator). Hence, all praise is for Allah.
This is the meaning of 'al-ḥamdu lillāh'.
5
Appreciate the eloquence of the Fatihah
Sanusi highlights the beauty of placing 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' before 'Rabb al-'ālamīn'. Reflect on how the argument demonstrates the harmony between meaning and expression.
This shows the Qur'anic miracle.

The Proof from Contingency and the Three Rational Judgments
Sanusi states the essential attribute of existence and presents the contingency argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof for God's existence through contingency and the foundational role of the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz).

Core Idea
Contingent things point to a Necessary One.

Sanusi begins the theological section by establishing that existence is an essential attribute of God. He then provides a concise proof: because the universe is contingent and needs a determiner, a Necessary Existence must exist. This lesson unpacks that proof and shows why the three rational categories—necessary, impossible, possible—are crucial for theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: essential attribute, proof, then the three categories.
The passage has some unclear words marked [?]; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the proof steps
Find the claim that God's existence is necessary. Then find the reason: contingent things depend on Him, and their contingency is proven by their need for a determiner.
The proof is a concise version of the kalam cosmological argument.
3
Connect to the three rational judgments
Sanusi says understanding wajib, mustahil, ja'iz makes it easy to know what is necessary for God. Reflect on how these categories underpin the proof.
The categories are the grammar of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في [؟] في الالهيات الصفة النفسية : صفة الوجود له [؟] فإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم : أنَّه يجب لمولانا جل وعزَّ الوجود ، لتوقف وجودِ الحوادث على وجودِهِ تعالى ودليل حدوثها (١) : لزومُها (٢) لِمَا يَفتقرُ إلى المخصص يعني : أنَّكَ إذا تصوّرت معنى الواجب والمستحيل والجائز.. سَهُلَ عليكَ حينَئِذٍ معرفةُ ما يجبُ لمولانا جلَّ وعلا مِنَ الكمالاتِ ؛ إذ الحكم بوجوبها لمولانا جلَّ وعزَّ فرعُ تَصورِ معنى الواجب(۳) ، وقد عُرِفَ ممَّا سبق (1) فمما يجب عقلاً لمولانا تبارك وتعالى : الوجود (٤) ، وهذا يعني : الأجرام.
The discourse on [?] in theology. The essential attribute: the attribute of existence for Him [?]. So if you have known this, then know: that existence is necessary for our Master, Majestic and Exalted, because the existence of contingent things depends on His existence, and the proof of their contingency (1) is their being subject to change (2) due to what needs a determiner. That is: if you conceive the meaning of the necessary, the impossible, and the possible, then it becomes easy for you to know what is necessary for our Master, Majestic and Exalted, of perfections; for the judgment of their necessity for our Master, Majestic and Exalted, is a branch of conceiving the meaning of the necessary (3), and it has been known from what preceded. So from what is rationally necessary for our Master, Blessed and Exalted, is existence (4), and this means: the [heavenly] bodies.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage begins a section on divine attributes. The author first mentions the essential attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya) of existence (al-wujud) for God. He then presents the core proof: existence is necessary (wajib) for God because the existence of contingent things (al-hawadith) depends on Him. The proof of their contingency (dafil al-huduth) is that they are subject to change (luzumuha) and thus require a determiner (mukhassis). The author emphasizes that understanding the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) is foundational for knowing what perfections are necessary for God. He concludes that existence is one of the rationally necessary attributes of God, and notes that this applies to 'the bodies' (al-ajram) meaning all contingent entities.

الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
دليل الحدوث
المخصص
الأجرام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof of contingency is purely empirical, but Sanusi links it to the rational categories of wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz, emphasizing conceptual clarity.
Correction: Sanusi states that conceiving the meaning of the three categories is essential for judging what is necessary for God, showing that the proof is grounded in rational categories, not just observation.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the proof that God's existence is necessary?
A) The universe is designed
B) The universe had a beginning and thus needs a cause
C) The contingency of the universe and its need for a determiner
D) The ontological argument

Key Takeaway
The contingency of the world points to a Necessary Being whose existence is rationally necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your existence is contingent and dependent on God every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized that your being depends on a determiner?
?
The passage says understanding the three categories makes it easy to know God's perfections. What is holding you back from truly knowing God? Is it a lack of conceptual clarity or a lack of sincerity?
?
If existence is necessary for God, but your existence is possible, how does that affect your sense of humility and reliance on God? Write one specific way you will acknowledge your contingency today.

The Essential Attribute and the Three Rational Judgments
Sanusi defines the essential attribute and explains the necessity of understanding the three rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the essential attribute and why the three rational judgments are foundational for knowing God's necessary perfections.

Core Idea
Understanding necessary, impossible, and possible is the key to all Kalam reasoning about God.

Sanusi begins his theological discussion by defining the essential attribute (الصفة النفسية) as existence by itself. He then asserts that God's existence is necessary, evident from the dependency of contingent events. However, to properly assess what is necessary for God, one must first grasp the three rational categories: necessary, impossible, and possible. This passage establishes that conceptual foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of essential attribute
The passage opens by defining الصفة النفسية. Read it carefully and note that it refers to existence by itself.
2
Understand the necessity of God's existence
Sanusi states that God's existence is necessary because contingent events depend on Him. The evidence is their need for a specifier.
3
Identify the three rational judgments
The passage mentions الواجب, المستحيل, والجائز. Understand that these are the three categories that enable knowledge of God's perfections.
4
Connect to theoretical necessity
Note that knowledge of God's existence is a theoretical necessary, requiring reflection. This sets the stage for the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الا المينا في الالهيات الصفة النفسية : صفة الوجود له بخانه فإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم : أنَّه يجب لمولانا جل وعزَّ الوجود ، لتوقف وجودِ الحوادث على وجودِهِ تعالى ودليل حدوثها : لزومُها لِمَا يَفتقرُ إلى المخصص يعني : أنَّكَ إذا تصوّرت معنى الواجب والمستحيل والجائز.. سَهُلَ عليكَ حينَئِذٍ معرفةُ ما يجبُ لمولانا جلَّ وعلا مِنَ الكمالات ؛ إذ الحكم بوجوبها لمولانا جلَّ وعزَّ فرعُ تَصورِ معنى الواجب، وقد عُرِفَ ممَّا سبق فمما يجب عقلاً لمولانا تبارك وتعالى : الوجود ، وهذا يعني : الأجرام . الواجبُ مِنَ القسم الثاني مِنْ قسمي الواجب العقلي ؛ وهو الواجب النظري ، فتتوقَّفُ معرفتُهُ بحسب ما أجرى الله تعالى به العادة على النظر العقلي وذلك : أن تنظر في كلَّ ما سواه تبارك وتعالى ، فتجده أجراماً ؛ أي : مقادير تشغلُ الفراغ ، يأخذُ مِنَ الفراغ كلُّ واحدٍ منها قدْرَ ذَاتِهِ طُولاً وعَرْضاً ، وصفاتٍ تقومُ بها ؛ مِنْ ألوان وأكوان وغيرهما وما مِنْ لون أو كون أو غيرهما إلا وهو جائز يصح وجوده وعدمُهُ ؛ بدليل مشاهدة الأمرينِ فيهِ في كثيرٍ مِنَ الأجرام ، وما لم نشاهده فحكمه حكم ما شاهدناه ؛ لاستواء الجميع في حقيقةِ الجِرْميَّةِ . وكذلكَ : ما مِنْ مقدار مخصوص للحِرْمِ فِي الطُّولِ أَوِ العَرْضِ إِلا وهو جائز يقبلُ الوجود والعدم ؛ بأن يوجد ما هو أكبر منه أو أصغر ، إلا أن يكون تناهى في الصغر إلى مقدارِ الجوهر الفرد ؛ وهو المقدار الذي لا يقبل التجزئة لا حِسّاً ولا عقلاً ، فيقبلُ حينئذ مقدارُهُ العدم ؛ بأن يوجد ما أكبر منه ، لا بأن يوجد ما هو أصغر منه ؛ إذ لا أصغر منه . هو وقبول كلِّ مقدار مخصوص وكلٌّ صفة من صفاته للوجودِ والعدم .. هو لازم ذاتي له ، لا يمكن انفكاكه عنه ضرورةً ، وهذان الأمران المقبولانِ - وهما الوجود والعدم - متساويان في القبول لا ترجيح لأحدهما على الآخرِ مِنْ حيث ذاته فالوجود ليس أمراً اعتبارياً يعتبره المعتبر كأبوة زيد ، فالله تعالى واجب الوجود ، لا يقبل الانتفاء أزلاً وأبداً .
Speech concerning the divine matters. The essential attribute: the attribute of existence for Him by Himself. So if you have understood this, then know: that existence is necessary for our Master, Mighty and Majestic, because the existence of contingent events depends upon His existence, and the evidence for their occurrence [is]: their being inseparable from that which needs a specifier, meaning: that when you conceive the meaning of the necessary, the impossible, and the possible, then it becomes easy for you to know what is necessary for our Master, Mighty and Exalted, of perfections; for the judgment that they are necessary for our Master, Mighty and Majestic, is a branch of conceiving the meaning of the necessary. And it has been known from what preceded. Thus, among that which is rationally necessary for our Master, Blessed and Exalted, is existence, and this means: bodies. The necessary is of the second section of the two divisions of the rational necessary; and it is the theoretical necessary, so knowledge of it depends, according to the custom that Allah has established, upon rational reflection. That is: that you look at everything other than Him, Blessed and Exalted, and you find it to be bodies, i.e., quantities that occupy space, each one of them taking from space the measure of its own length and breadth, and attributes subsisting in them, such as colors, locations, and others. And there is no color or location or other than these except that it is possible, its existence and non-existence being valid; evidence being the observation of both states in many bodies, and what we have not observed has the same ruling as what we have observed, because of the uniformity of all bodies in the reality of corporeality. Similarly, there is no specific quantity of a body in length or width except that it is possible, accepting existence and non-existence, in that something larger or smaller than it may exist, unless it has reached the utmost smallness to the measure of the indivisible atom; and that is the measure that does not admit division, neither sensibly nor rationally. Then its measure accepts non-existence in that something larger than it exists, not in that something smaller than it exists, for there is nothing smaller than it. And the acceptance of every specific quantity and every attribute of its attributes for existence and non-existence is an essential necessary property of it, inseparable from it by necessity. And these two accepted states—which are existence and non-existence—are equal in acceptance, with no preponderance of one over the other from the perspective of its essence. Thus existence is not a conceptual matter that one conceptualizes like the fatherhood of Zayd. So Allah, Exalted is He, is necessary of existence, not accepting annihilation eternally and forever.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and classification. It begins with a brief definition: the essential attribute (الصفة النفسية) is the attribute of existence for God by Himself. Then it states that the existence of God is necessary because contingent events depend on Him for their existence. The evidence for the occurrence of these events is their inseparability from that which needs a specifier (مخصص). The author then explains that understanding the three rational judgments—necessary, impossible, and possible—facilitates knowledge of what perfections are necessary for God. He clarifies that knowing God's existence is a theoretical necessary (واجب نظري), which requires rational reflection. This reflection consists of examining everything other than God: we find them to be bodies (أجرام), which are quantities occupying space, and they have attributes like colors and locations. All such attributes are possible (جائز), as observed in many bodies. What we have not observed follows the same ruling due to the uniform nature of corporeality. Similarly, specific quantities of bodies are possible, admitting larger or smaller sizes, except for the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد), which is the smallest measure that cannot be divided. This atom admits non-existence only by something larger existing, not smaller. The acceptance of existence and non-existence is an essential property of these bodies and attributes, and the two states are equally possible with no inherent preference. Hence, existence is not merely a conceptual notion; rather, God is necessarily existent, not subject to non-existence at any time. The passage concludes with the affirmation that God is the necessary existent, unendingly and eternally.

Key Terms
الصفة النفسية
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's existence is self-evident without need for rational categories.
Correction: Sanusi shows that understanding the three rational judgments is essential for properly establishing God's necessary attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the essential attribute (الصفة النفسية)?
A) The attribute of existence for God by Himself
B) The attribute of knowledge
C) The attribute of power
D) The attribute of will

Key Takeaway
The essential attribute is existence by itself, and understanding the three rational categories is the foundation for all theological judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if your very existence depends on the Necessary Being every moment? What would change in your daily routine if you constantly remembered that you are merely possible and could cease to exist at any instant?
?
When you consider the things you rely on—health, wealth, relationships—are you aware that they are all possible beings? How does that awareness reshape your attachment to them and your need for the One who is Necessary?
?
This lesson says that understanding the three categories is the key to knowing what is necessary for God. Have you ever deeply pondered what is required of you because God is Necessary—such as perfect worship, gratitude, and reliance? What step will you take today to align your life with that reality?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
Proving God's Existence from the Contingency of Bodies and Accidents
Sanusi provides the detailed rational proof for God's existence by examining the contingency of bodies and their attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's existence based on the contingency of bodies, their attributes, and the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Everything other than God is contingent, requiring a necessary existent to bring it into being.

Having established the need to understand the three rational judgments, Sanusi now provides the detailed proof. He argues that all entities other than God are bodies (أجرام) with attributes that are possible, meaning they can exist or not exist. Since they have no inherent preference for existence, they require an external specifier. This proof culminates in affirming God as the necessary existent, who is not subject to non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of theoretical necessary
The passage explains that knowledge of God's existence is a theoretical necessary (واجب نظري), requiring rational reflection on creation.
2
Examine bodies and their attributes
Look at everything other than God: they are bodies with attributes like colors. All these attributes are possible, as we observe them changing.
3
Consider specific quantities and the indivisible atom
Quantities of bodies are also possible, except the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) which cannot be smaller. Even it accepts non-existence by being larger.
4
Realize the equal acceptance of existence and non-existence
All bodies and attributes equally accept existence and non-existence, with no inherent preference. This indicates they need a specifier (God).
5
Conclude that God is necessary existent
Therefore, existence is not a mere concept; God is necessary of existence, not accepting annihilation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
Contingency of Bodies and Their Attributes
Sanusi introduces the contingency of bodies and their attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all bodies and their attributes are contingent, and that this contingency points to the necessary existence of Allah.

Core Idea
All bodies and their attributes are equally possible, so their existence requires a necessary cause.

Sanusi begins this foundational proof by asking us to examine everything other than Allah. We find they are bodies with attributes, and every attribute and specific magnitude is contingent—it can either exist or not. This observation, coupled with the principle that what we observe applies also to what we don't, sets the stage for the argument that only a Necessary Being can explain why things exist rather than not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the definition
Sanusi defines 'theoretical obligation' (al-wajib al-nazari) and states that knowledge of it requires rational consideration.
This is the starting point for the argument.
3
Observe the two types of contingency
He points out that both attributes (like colors) and specific magnitudes are contingent. Note the exception of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard).
4
Understand the essential nature of contingency
The acceptance of existence and non-existence is an essential, inseparable property of bodies. This is a key logical point.
5
Conclude with Allah's necessity
Sanusi concludes that Allah is necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), not accepting non-existence. This sets up the next lesson's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجبُ مِنَ القسم الثاني مِنْ قسمي الواجب العقلي؛ وهو الواجب النظري، فتتوقَّفُ معرفتُهُ بحسب ما أجرى الله تعالى به العادة على النظر العقلي. وذلك: أن تنظر في كلَّ ما سواه تبارك وتعالى، فتجده أجراماً؛ أي: مقادير تشغلُ الفراغ، يأخذُ مِنَ الفراغ كلُّ واحدٍ منها قدْرَ ذَاتِهِ طُولاً وعَرْضاً، وصفاتٍ تقومُ بها؛ مِنْ ألوان وأكوان وغيرهما. وما مِنْ لون أو كون أو غيرهما إلا وهو جائز يصح وجوده وعدمُهُ؛ بدليل مشاهدة الأمرينِ فيهِ في كثيرٍ مِنَ الأجرام، وما لم نشاهده فحكمه حكم ما شاهدناه؛ لاستواء الجميع في حقيقةِ الجِرْميَّةِ. وكذلكَ: ما مِنْ مقدار مخصوص للحِرْمِ فِي الطُّولِ أَوِ العَرْضِ إِلا وهو جائز يقبلُ الوجود والعدم؛ بأن يوجد ما هو أكبر منه أو أصغر، إلا أن يكون تناهى في الصغر إلى مقدارِ الجوهر الفرد؛ وهو المقدار الذي لا يقبل التجزئة لا حِسّاً ولا عقلاً، فيقبلُ حينئذ مقدارُهُ العدم؛ بأن يوجد ما أكبر منه، لا بأن يوجد ما هو أصغر منه؛ إذ لا أصغر منه. هو وقبول كلِّ مقدار مخصوص وكلٌّ صفة من صفاته للوجودِ والعدم .. هو لازم ذاتي له، لا يمكن انفكاكه عنه ضرورةً، وهذان الأمران المقبولانِ - وهما الوجود والعدم - متساويان في القبول لا ترجيح لأحدهما على الآخرِ مِنْ حيث ذاته. فالوجود ليس أمراً اعتبارياً يعتبره المعتبر كأبوة زيد، فالله تعالى واجب الوجود، لا يقبل الانتفاء أزلاً وأبداً. فإذاً؛ يستحيل عقلاً أن يكونَ جِرْمٌ مِنَ الأجرام أو صفةٌ مِنْ صفاتِهِ قديماً لم يسبق وجودَهُ عدمٌ؛ لِمَا يلزمُ عليهِ مِنْ ترجُحِ وجودِ المقدارِ المخصوص الجائز على عدمِهِ المساوي له في الجواز، وترجُحِ وجودِ صفتِهِ المخصوصة الجائزة على مقابلها بلا مُرجّح، وذلك جمع بينَ متنافيين؛ وهما الاستواء والرجحان، وذلك لا يُعقَلُ. [ العوالم كلُّها بحدوثها ناطقة بوجوب وجودِهِ تعالى ] فإذاً؛ قد دلّ كلُّ ما سوى مولانا تبارك وتعالى مِنْ جهةٍ مقدارِهِ المخصوص وصفتِهِ المخصوصة .. على أمرين: أحدهما: وجوبُ وجودِ المولى تبارك وتعالى؛ ليرجح بإرادتِهِ مقدارَ كلَّ جِرْمٍ وصفته المخصوصينِ بهِ على مقابلهما، ويُوجِدَ ما شاءَ مِنْ ذلكَ على وَفْقِ إرادتِهِ. الثاني: الحدوث لكلِّ جِرْمِ وصفاتِهِ؛ لِمَا ثبتَ مِنْ طريقِ الجواز وجوب افتقارها للفاعل؛ لأنَّ القديم لا يكون إلا واجباً غنياً عن الفاعل.
The obligatory from the second division of the two divisions of rational obligation: it is the theoretical obligation. Its knowledge depends, according to what Allah Almighty has made customary, on rational consideration. That is: you look at everything other than Him, Blessed and Exalted, and you find it to be bodies (ajram), i.e., magnitudes that occupy empty space; each of them takes from empty space the measure of its own essence in length and breadth, and attributes (sifat) that subsist in them, such as colors, locations (akwan), and others. And there is no color, location, or other than them except that it is contingent (ja'iz), its existence and non-existence being valid, as evidenced by observing both states in many bodies. And what we have not observed, its ruling is the same as what we have observed, because all are equal in the reality of corporeality. Likewise, there is no specific magnitude for a body in length or breadth except that it is contingent, accepting existence and non-existence, by the existence of what is larger or smaller than it, unless it has reached the limit of smallness to the magnitude of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), which is the magnitude that does not admit division, neither sensibly nor intellectually. Then its magnitude accepts non-existence by the existence of what is larger than it, not by the existence of what is smaller, for there is nothing smaller than it. And the acceptance of every specific magnitude and every attribute of its attributes for existence and non-existence is an essential necessary property (lazim dhati) for it, impossible to separate from it necessarily. And these two accepted states – existence and non-existence – are equal in acceptance, with no preference (tarjih) of one over the other in terms of its essence. Therefore, existence is not a relational matter considered by the considerer, like Zayd's fatherhood. So Allah Almighty is necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), not accepting non-existence eternally and everlastingly. Thus, it is rationally impossible for a body of the bodies or an attribute of its attributes to be eternal (qadim), not preceded by non-existence, because it would entail the preponderance (tarjuh) of the existence of the specific contingent magnitude over its non-existence equal to it in contingency, and the preponderance of the existence of its specific contingent attribute over its opposite without a preponderator (murajjih). And that is a combination of two contradictory things: equality and preponderance, and that is not rationally intelligible. [All worlds, by their temporal origination, speak of the necessity of His existence.] Thus, everything other than our Lord, Blessed and Exalted, from the aspect of its specific magnitude and its specific attribute, has indicated two matters: First: the necessity of the existence of our Lord, Blessed and Exalted, so that by His will He may give preponderance to the specific magnitude and attribute of each body over their opposites, and bring into existence what He wills of that in accordance with His will. Second: the temporal origination (huduth) of every body and its attributes, because it has been established through the way of contingency the obligation of their need for a maker (fa'il), for the eternal is only necessary and independent of a maker.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of God and the temporal origination of the world, based on the contingency (jawaz) of bodies and their attributes. The author begins by defining the type of obligation under discussion: it is the theoretical obligation (al-wajib al-nazari), knowledge of which requires rational consideration. He then instructs the reader to observe all things other than God, finding them to be bodies (ajram), i.e., magnitudes occupying space, with attributes like colors and locations. He asserts that every such attribute is contingent (ja'iz), meaning it can exist or not exist, as observed in many bodies. For those not observed, the same judgment applies because all share the reality of corporeality. Similarly, every specific magnitude (except the indivisible atom) is contingent, accepting existence and non-existence through the possibility of larger or smaller magnitudes. The indivisible atom is the smallest magnitude, accepting non-existence only through a larger magnitude. This acceptance of existence and non-existence is an essential necessary property (lazim dhati) of bodies, inseparable from them. The two states, existence and non-existence, are equal in possibility; neither is preferable in itself. Therefore, existence is not a mere relational concept like fatherhood; thus Allah is necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), incapable of non-existence. From this, it follows that no body or attribute can be eternal (qadim), because that would require a preference (tarjih) for existence over non-existence without a preponderator (murajjih), which is rationally impossible as it combines equality and preference. The text then concludes by stating that everything other than God indicates two things: first, the necessity of God's existence as the One who preponderates existence by His will; second, the temporal origination (huduth) of every body and its attributes, since their contingency shows their need for a maker, whereas the eternal is self-sufficient.

جوهر فرد

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) accepts non-existence only through a larger magnitude, not a smaller one?
A) Because it is the smallest possible magnitude, so nothing smaller can exist.
B) Because it is composed of parts that can be divided.
C) Because its existence is necessary.
D) Because it is eternal.

Key Takeaway
All bodies and their attributes are essentially contingent, meaning they could be otherwise, which points to a necessary cause for their existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just understood that every atom of your being could have not existed. Does this reality penetrate your heart, or do you still live as though your existence is self-evident? How will you let this truth transform your sense of absolute need for Allah today?
?
If Allah ceased to sustain your existence for even a moment, you would vanish. Does this make you feel anxious or secure? What specific step will you take today to rely on Him as the only Necessary Existence?
?
The proof shows that your own existence is not a mere tag but a real gift from the Necessary One. Are there areas of your life where you act as if your successes are self-made? How can you cultivate a constant, tangible awareness that you are contingent and He is necessary?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
5 min
The Preponderance Principle and the Necessity of a Creator
Sanusi derives the impossibility of eternal contingency and concludes God's existence and the world's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the impossibility of eternal contingency forces the existence of a preponderator, proving God's existence and the world's temporal origin.

Core Idea
If a contingent thing were eternal, existence would be preferred over non-existence without reason—an absurdity.

Having established that all bodies and attributes are contingent, Sanusi now shows that no contingent being can be eternal. For if it were, its existence would have to be preferred over non-existence without any cause, which is rationally impossible. This leads to two crucial conclusions: there must be a Necessary Being who preponderates, and all bodies and their attributes are temporally originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and find the contradiction
Read the Arabic and translation. Look for the statement that if a body were eternal, it would combine equality and preference.
2
Understand the principle of preponderance
Sanusi argues that existence and non-existence are equal in possibility; a preference (tarjih) requires a preponderator (murajjih).
3
Identify the two conclusions
The passage ends with two conclusions: God exists as the Preponderator, and all bodies and attributes are temporally originated (hadith).
4
Reflect on the necessity of the argument
Why is it that a contingent being cannot be eternal? Because its existence would lack a cause, contradicting the equality of possibility.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction

Why does Sanusi argue that no body or attribute can be eternal?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every detail of your existence—your body, your mind, your circumstances—is the result of Allah's specific preponderance at every moment, why do you sometimes live as if you are self-sufficient? How would your day change if you woke up thinking, 'At every instant, Allah chooses for me to exist with these exact attributes'?
?
The argument proves that without a Preponderator, there would be no reason for you to be you rather than someone else. Does this realization fill you with gratitude for the specific life Allah has given you, or with a sense of responsibility? What does it mean for how you use your unique attributes?
?
If you truly internalized that your very existence is a constant act of divine preference, would you still feel entitled to anything—health, wealth, respect? What would it mean to live in complete humility, knowing that at any moment Allah could choose to give you non-existence instead of existence?

Allah's Existence
beginner

The Impossibility of an Eternal Impediment or Condition
Sanusi refutes the possibility of an eternal impediment or missing condition for contingent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an eternal impediment or missing condition cannot prevent the existence of contingent things, and how this supports the argument for a necessary creator.

Core Idea
An eternal impediment can never cease, and an eternal condition can never arise—so contingency demands creation.

Sanusi now responds to an objection that might be raised against the argument from contingency. The objector might say: perhaps the existence of the world was prevented by some eternal impediment or depended on a condition that was eternally absent. Sanusi shows that both possibilities lead to absurd conclusions, thereby reinforcing the need for a necessary creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow through before you interpret.
Notice how Sanusi presents two parallel scenarios.
2
Understand the two scenarios
Identify the two cases: an eternal impediment (مانع أزلي) and a missing eternal condition (شرط أزلي منتفٍ). Sanusi treats each separately.
3
Follow the logical consequences
For the impediment: if it is eternal, it can never cease, so nothing would ever exist. For the condition: if it is eternally absent, it could never come to be without another eternal impediment or infinite regress.
Both conclusions are impossible, so the objection fails.
4
Connect to the overall argument
Realize that this refutation strengthens the proof for a necessary creator: contingent things exist, so there cannot be an eternal impediment or missing condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالجواب : أنَّهُ لو وُجِدَ مانع مِنْ وجود العوالم في الأزلِ .. لَمَا انتفى أبداً ؛ لأنَّ ما ثبت قدمُهُ استحالَ عدمُهُ ، فيلزم ألا يوجد شيءٌ مِنَ العوالم أبداً ، ولو انتفى شرط وجودِ العوالم في الأزلِ .. لَمَا وُجِدَ ذلك الشرط أبداً ، فلا يوجد أيضاً شيءٌ مِنَ العوالم أبداً ؛ لأنَّ وجودَ ذلك الشرط فيما لا يزالُ .. مُتوقِّفٌ على انتفاء مانع أزلي ، أو تسلسل شرائط إلى غير أول ، وكلاهما محال
The answer: that if there were an impediment to the existence of the worlds in eternity, it would never cease; because what is established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible, so it would follow that nothing of the worlds would ever exist. And if the condition for the existence of the worlds were absent in eternity, that condition would never exist, so nothing of the worlds would ever exist either; because the existence of that condition in what is yet to come depends on the removal of an eternal impediment, or an infinite regress of conditions, both of which are impossible.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The author argues that the existence of contingent things cannot be prevented by an eternal impediment nor dependent on an eternal condition, because both lead to the impossibility of any contingency. The reasoning: if there were an eternal impediment, it would never cease, so nothing would ever exist; if an eternal condition were missing, it would never come to be, so again nothing would exist. Thus, contingency proves the existence of a necessary creator.

Key Terms
مانع
شرط
أزلي
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that an eternal impediment could eventually be removed or that a condition missing in eternity could arise later without cause.
Correction: An eternal thing cannot cease to exist; an eternally absent condition cannot begin to exist without an impossible removal of an eternal impediment or an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would happen if there were an eternal impediment to the existence of the worlds?
A) The worlds would exist after the impediment is removed.
B) Nothing would ever exist.
C) The impediment would cease eventually.
D) The worlds would exist in eternity.

Key Takeaway
An eternal impediment cannot cease, and a missing eternal condition cannot arise—so contingent existence demands a necessary creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If an eternal impediment cannot be removed, and an eternally absent condition cannot arise, what does that mean for your own reliance on God as the sole creator? Are there any 'impediments' in your heart—like pride or doubt—that you treat as eternal, refusing to submit to the Creator?
?
This argument shows that nothing contingent can exist without a necessary cause. How does this truth confront your moments of self-sufficiency? When you achieve success, do you truly acknowledge that without God's condition being met, you would not exist at all?
?
Sanusi eliminates infinite regress as impossible. In your own life, is there any area where you are waiting for an endless chain of conditions to be met before turning to God? What first step must you take today to break that chain and depend solely on Him?

Preponderator Required for Specific Determinations
Sanusi extends the proof of a preponderator to specific determinations of contingent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that not only existence but also specific determinations like time, place, direction, quantity, and quality require a preponderator.

Core Idea
Every specific determination of a contingent thing demands a preponderator.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi established that the existence of any contingent thing requires a preponderator to tip the scales from non-existence to existence. Now, he extends that reasoning to the specific attributes of the contingent thing—its time, place, direction, and, if it is a body, its quantity and quality. Without a preponderator, any specific determination would be just as impossible as existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi builds on the earlier proof by adding 'and likewise' (وكذلك) to show the parallel reasoning.
2
Identify the specific determinations listed
The passage mentions time, place, direction, quantity, and quality. Mark each one. Note that quantity and quality are conditional on the thing being a body ('if they are bodies').
These are the attributes that require a preponderator.
3
Understand the logic of preponderance
For each determination, the contingent thing could have been otherwise. The fact that it has this specific determination rather than its opposite requires an external cause (preponderator) to tilt the balance.
This is the core argument of the passage.
4
Connect to the broader proof
Realize that this extends the argument for the necessity of a Creator. The preponderator for all these determinations is ultimately Allah.
The commentary will explain this connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يستحيل ترجُحُ زمن وجودها المخصوص ومكانها المخصوص وجهتها المخصوصة على ما يقابلها بلا مُرجّح وكذلكَ يستحيلُ ترجُحُ مقدارها المخصوص وصفتها المخصوصة إن كانَتْ أجراماً على ما يقابلها مِنْ غيرِ مُرجح موجود
And likewise, it is impossible for the preponderance of their specific time of existence and their specific place and their specific direction over what is opposite to them, without a preponderator. And likewise, it is impossible for the preponderance of their specific quantity and their specific quality, if they are bodies, over what is opposite to them, without an existing preponderator.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an extension of the proof for the necessity of a preponderator. After establishing that existence preponderates over non-existence only with a preponderator, the author now applies the same reasoning to the specific determinations of contingent things. For any contingent existent, its particular time, place, and direction require a preponderator to select them from their opposites. Additionally, for bodies, their specific quantity and quality also require a preponderator. Without an external preponderator, any specific determination would be arbitrary and impossible.

The Agent Must Be Necessary in Existence
Sanusi argues that the agent of contingent things must have necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the agent of contingent beings must have necessary existence, not merely possible existence.

Core Idea
A possible agent would itself need a cause, so only a necessary being suffices.

This brief but powerful passage from Sanusi refines the classic causal argument for God's existence. It argues that contingent things cannot depend on an agent whose own existence is merely possible, for that would lead to an infinite regress. Instead, the ultimate agent must be necessary in every respect.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: conditional dependence, then elimination of the possible option.
The passage references an upcoming proof of eternity (burhan al-qidam).
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that contingent things depend on the agent's existence being necessary, not on its existence as such. Find the key phrases.
3
Understand the argument against a possible agent
The author says that if the agent's existence were merely possible, that would entail its impossibility. This is a condensed version of the proof of eternity argument.
Think about why a possible being cannot be self-sufficient.
4
Connect to the broader theology
This argument establishes that only a necessary being (Allah) can be the ultimate cause of the universe. This is foundational for later proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنَّما توقف وجودُ الحوادثِ على كون وجودِ فاعلها واجباً ، لا على مطلق وجودِهِ وإِنْ كانَ جائزاً؛ لأنَّ تقدير جواز الوجودِ لهُ يستلزم استحالة الوجودِ له على ما يأتي في برهان القِدَم ، فتعيَّن أن يكون وجودها موقوفاً على كونِ وجودِ فاعلها واجباً لا جائزاً
The existence of contingent things depends only on the fact that the existence of their agent is necessary, not on the absolute existence of it even if it is possible; because supposing that existence is possible for it entails the impossibility of existence for it, as will come in the proof of eternity. So it is determined that their existence depends on the fact that the existence of their agent is necessary, not possible.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage refines the causal argument for God's existence. The author states that contingent things require an agent whose existence is necessary (wajib al-wujud), not merely possible (ja'iz al-wujud). If the agent were merely possible, it would itself be contingent and would require another preponderator, leading to an infinite regress or circularity. The author references the upcoming proof of eternity (burhan al-qidam) which will show that a possible being cannot be the ultimate cause. Therefore, the only viable conclusion is that the agent must be necessary in existence.

برهان القِدَم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a possible agent could suffice as the ultimate cause of contingent things.
Correction: The author argues that if the agent's existence were merely possible, it would be contingent and require a preponderator, leading to infinite regress or circularity. Thus the agent must be necessary.

Checkpoint

C) Because the proof of eternity shows all agents are necessary.
D) Because contingent things cannot depend on a possible agent.

Key Takeaway
The ultimate cause of contingent things must be a being whose existence is necessary, not merely possible.

The Necessity of the Agent's Existence
Sanusi argues that contingent dependence implies the agent's necessary existence and critiques Mu'tazilite secondary causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingent existence requires a necessary agent and how this argument distinguishes orthodox Sunni theology from Mu'tazilite views on secondary causation.

Core Idea
Contingent existence cannot depend on another contingent; only a necessary being can be the ultimate cause.

This lesson presents a key argument in kalam: that the existence of contingent things (al-hawadith) requires their agent to be necessary in existence (wajib al-wujud). Sanusi then applies this argument to critique the Mu'tazila, who affirmed secondary causation while acknowledging origination. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping Sunni proofs for God's necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Notice the compact style and the presence of a footnote.
2
Identify the main claim and reasoning
The passage states that contingent existence depends on the agent being necessary, not merely possible. Identify the conditional reasoning: if the agent were possible, its existence would be impossible (as will be proven in the proof of pre-eternity).
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument: a possible agent leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand the implication for secondary causation
Sanusi adds a footnote about groups like the Mu'tazila who affirm that created things can be agents. He calls their view a 'heinous innovation' (bid'ah shani'ah) but clarifies that it does not amount to denial of God's pre-existence (juhud).
This shows the nuanced distinction between innovation and disbelief in Sunni theology.
4
Reflect on the overall lesson
Summarize the key point: the necessity of the First Cause is established, and any theory that attributes causal efficacy to contingent beings is rejected as innovation, but not as disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنَّما توقف وجودُ الحوادثِ على كون وجودِ فاعلها واجباً ، لا على مطلق وجودِهِ وإِنْ كانَ جائزاً ؛ لأنَّ تقدير جواز الوجودِ لهُ يستلزم استحالة الوجودِ له على ما يأتي في برهان القِدَم (۱) ، فتعيَّن أن يكون وجودها موقوفاً على كونِ وجودِ فاعلها واجباً لا جائزاً (۲) (۱) سيأتي ( ص ١٤٨ ) . (۲) وبهذا ينجو القائلون بتأثير ما سواه تعالى مع إقرارهم بحدوثه ؛ كالمعتزلة القائلين بالتولد والاعتماد وخلق العبد أفعال نفسه ، فيكون قولهم بدعة شنيعة لا تقتضي جحود القديم سبحانه
And indeed the existence of contingent things depends on the existence of their agent being necessary, not on the absolute existence of it even if it is possible; because supposing possibility of existence to it entails impossibility of existence to it, as will come in the proof of pre-eternity (1), so it is determined that their existence depends on the existence of their agent being necessary, not possible (2). (1) It will come (p. 148). (2) And by this, those who affirm the effect of other than Him, may He be exalted, while acknowledging its origination, such as the Mu'tazila who affirm generated acts (tawallud) and acquisition (i'timad) and the slave creating his own actions, so their statement is a heinous innovation (bid'ah shani'ah) that does not entail denial (juhud) of the Ancient, may He be glorified.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, an argument that the agent of contingent things must be necessary in existence (wajib al-wujud). The reasoning is that if the agent were merely possible (ja'iz), it would itself require a preponderant for its existence, leading to an infinite regress or circularity. This is a standard proof for the necessity of the First Cause. The phrase 'as will come in the proof of pre-eternity' refers to a later section. The second part is a footnote that comments on groups like the Mu'tazila who affirm secondary causation while acknowledging the origination of the world. The note says that their view, though a heinous innovation, does not amount to denial of God's pre-existence. This is a nuanced point distinguishing between heresy (bid'a) and outright disbelief.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Mu'tazila's view on secondary causation makes them disbelievers.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly says their statement is a 'heinous innovation' that does not entail denial (juhud) of the Ancient God. Thus, it is a serious error but not disbelief.

A) Possible

D) Contingent

Key Takeaway
Contingent existence logically necessitates a necessary agent, and while secondary causation theories are innovations, they do not constitute disbelief in God's pre-eternity.

The Necessity of God's Eternity (Al-Qidam)
Sanusi proves God's eternity via circularity/regress and defines qidam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's eternity (qidam) is rationally necessary and how the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress proves it.

Core Idea
God must be eternal because originating Him leads to absurdity.

Sanusi begins with a fundamental claim: God must be eternal and everlasting. He immediately provides a proof: if God were not eternal, He would need an agent and be originated, which leads to either circularity or infinite regress—both impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument and defines what 'eternity' means in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if not eternal, then...'.
Pay attention to the footnote definitions.
2
Identify the conditional argument
The passage argues that if God were not eternal, He would be originated and thus require an agent. This leads to circularity or infinite regress.
The impossibility of dawr and tasalsul is key.
3
Understand the proof from circularity/regress
Sanusi states that the falsity of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) proves God's eternity. Reflect on why these are impossible.
These concepts are foundational in kalam.
4
Study the definition of qidam
Read the definition: qidam means not being receptive to non-existence in pre-eternity. The footnote gives three expressions for this.
The three expressions converge in meaning.
5
Review the footnote clarification
Al-Baylī explains that eternity is a necessary consequent of necessary existence, and distinguishes the three expressions.
This resolves potential confusion about the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب له تعالى القدم والبقاء، وإلا كان محتاجاً إلى الفاعل، فيكون حادثاً، فيجب له من العجز ما وجب لسائر الحوادث، بل يكون حينئذ وجوده مستحيلاً؛ لما يلزم على تقدير حدوثه من الدور أو التسلسل المستحيلين. بطلان الدور والتسلسل دليل على قدمه تعالى ثم على بقائه. يجب له تبارك وتعالى أن يكون غير قابل للعدم في الأزل؛ وهو معنى القدم (١)، ولا فيما لا يزال؛ وهو معنى البقاء (٢). يعني: (١) قال العلامة البيلي في تقييداته على شرح صغرى الصغرى (ق ٩): (القدم ذكر بعد الوجود من ذكر اللازم؛ لأن من وجب وجوده ثبت قدمه، والقدم في حق ذاته العلية وصفاته السنية قدم ذات؛ وهو نفي العدم السابق للوجود؛ أي: فوجوده تعالى لا يقبل العدم في الأزل، أو نفي الأولية للوجود؛ إذ القديم الذي لا أول له، فهو الأول، أو نفي افتتاح الوجود، والعبارات الثلاث متقاربة؛ لأن متعلقاتها مختلفة، فقول الهدهدي في «شرح العقيدة الصغرى»: «معناها واحد» أي: يرجع إلى المقصد في تعريف القدم، وليست متحدة مفهوماً وماصدقاً). (٢) قال العلامة البيلي في «تقييداته على شرح صغرى الصغرى» (ق ٩): إذ لو كان قابلاً للعدم لما كان واجب الوجود، بل كان جائز الوجود، وكل جائز الوجود فهو مفتقر إلى الفاعل كسائر الجائزات (١)، فيكون حادثاً مثلها، وذلك (٢) مستحيل لوجهين: أحدهما: أنه يلزم أن يكون عاجزاً كسائر الحوادث؛ المساواته لها في الحدوث والجواز، فلا يصح إسناد شيء من الحوادث إليه؛ لعموم العجز عن الإيجاد لكل حادث، وأيضاً: يلزم عجزه عن الإيجاد من أجل التمانع بينه وبين موجده الذي افتقر إليه، وأيضاً: إسناد الممكنات إليه بالخصوص دون موجده.. تخصيص بلا مخصص، وأيضاً: فليس استناد سائر الممكنات إليه بأولى من العكس. الثاني: أنه يلزم أن يكون وجوده حينئذ مستحيلاً لا يتصور في العقل ثبوته؛ لأنه إن قدر قبول ذاته للعدم صار جائزاً مفتقراً إلى الفاعل، ويلزم أن يكون فاعله جائزاً مفتقراً إلى الفاعل؛ لأنه مثله في الألوهية (٣)، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى فاعل الفاعل، ثم كذلك أبداً. فإن انتهى العدد وانحصر.. لزم الدور، فيلزم أن يكون الأول الذي انتهى إليه العدد إنما أوجده بعض من بعده ممن تأخر وجوده. (عطفه على القدم عطف لازم على ملزوم؛ لأن من ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، والمقصد التنصيص على كل صفة بعينها؛ لأن المقام مقام اعتقادي) (١) لأن ثبوت العلة يستلزم ثبوت المعلول (٢) يعني: جميع ما تقدم؛ من قبول العدم والجواز والافتقار والحدوث (٣) يعني: على سبيل الفرض و
And it is necessary for Him, exalted, eternity and everlastingness. Otherwise, He would be in need of an agent, so He would be originated, and then there would be incumbent on Him from incapacity what is incumbent on other originated things; rather, then His existence would be impossible, due to what follows from the supposition of His origination of circularity or infinite regress, both impossible. The falsity of circularity and infinite regress is a proof for His eternity, exalted, then for His everlastingness. It is necessary for Him, blessed and exalted, that He not be receptive to non-existence in pre-eternity—that is the meaning of eternity (1)—nor in what is everlasting—that is the meaning of everlastingness (2). Meaning: (1) al-Baylī said in his glosses on Sharh al-Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā (folio 9): “Eternity is mentioned after existence as a mention of the necessary consequent, because whoever’s existence is necessary, his eternity is established. Eternity in respect of His exalted essence and His elevated attributes is eternity of essence, and it is the negation of prior non-existence to existence, i.e., His existence, exalted, does not accept non-existence in pre-eternity, or the negation of primacy to existence, for the eternal is that which has no first, so He is the First, or the negation of the beginning of existence. The three expressions are close, because their referents differ. So al-Hudhdī said in Sharḥ al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā: ‘Their meaning is one’ meaning it returns to the intent in defining eternity, but they are not unified in concept and extension.” (2) al-Baylī said in his glosses on Sharḥ al-Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā (folio 9): “For if He were receptive to non-existence, He would not be necessary of existence, but rather would be possible of existence. And every possible of existence is in need of an agent like other possible things (1), so He would be originated like them. And that (2) is impossible for two reasons: The first: that it would entail that He be incapable like other originated things, due to His equality with them in origination and possibility, so it would not be correct to attribute anything of originated things to Him, due to the generality of incapability of bringing into existence for every originated thing. And also, His incapability of bringing into existence would be entailed because of the prevention (tamānuʿ) between Him and the one who brought Him into existence, to whom He is in need. And also, the attribution of possible things to Him specifically rather than to the one who brought Him into existence is a specification without a specifier. And also, the dependence of all other possible things on Him is not more likely than the opposite. The second: that it would entail that His existence then would be impossible, not conceivable in the intellect as established, because if His essence is supposed to accept non-existence, it becomes possible and in need of an agent, and it would be necessary that its agent be possible and in need of an agent, because it is like it in divinity (3). Then we transfer the discussion to the agent of that agent, and so on forever. If the number ends and is limited, circularity would be entailed, and then it would be necessary that the first, at which the number ends, was brought into existence by some of those after it, whose existence is later. (Its conjunction with eternity is a conjunction of a necessary consequence with its necessitating condition, because whoever’s eternity is established, his non-existence is impossible. And the intent is to specify each attribute distinctly, because the context is a context of belief.) (1) Because the establishment of the cause necessitates the establishment of the effect. (2) Meaning: all that preceded—from acceptance of non-existence, possibility, need, and origination. (3) Meaning: by way of assumption. And
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of God's eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa'). The author (Sanusi) begins by stating that eternity and everlastingness are necessary for God, then presents a proof: if God were not eternal, He would need an agent and thus be originated, which would entail incapacity and impossibility. The falsity of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) serves as evidence for His eternity. The passage then defines eternity as being non-receptive to non-existence in pre-eternity, and everlastingness as being non-receptive to non-existence in the future. Two extensive footnotes from al-Baylī clarify that eternity is a necessary consequent of necessary existence, and that the definitions of eternity are varied but converge on the same intent. The main argument then proves everlastingness: if God could cease to exist, He would be possible, thus needing an agent, leading to origination. This is impossible for two reasons. First, it would entail incapacity, as He would be like other originated things, unable to bring about events, and would conflict with His own agent (tamānuʿ). Second, it would lead to an infinite regress or circularity, making His existence impossible. The footnotes comment that the conjunction of everlastingness with eternity is a necessary consequence, and that the two reasons are based on the impossibility of a possible being existing without a cause. The passage concludes with the circularity argument: if a chain of agents ends, the first would be caused by a later one, which is absurd.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
حدوث (huduth)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
عجز (ajz)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The student might think the proof of eternity is circular because it assumes existence to prove eternity.
Correction: The footnote clarifies that eternity is a necessary consequent (lazim) of wajib al-wujud, not a separate premise.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what proves the necessity of God's eternity?
A) The definition of qidam
B) The falsity of circularity and infinite regress
C) The need for an agent
D) The incapacity argument

Key Takeaway
God's eternity is proven because any alternative leads to the absurdity of circularity or infinite regress.

?

?
The proof of God's eternity depends on the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress. Are there areas in your life where you are caught in a cycle of seeking fulfillment from created things? How does knowing that only the Eternal One can break that cycle change your next step?
?
The definition of qidam is 'not being receptive to non-existence in pre-eternity.' Meditate on the fact that your existence is entirely borrowed, while God's existence is self-necessary. How does this contrast affect your sense of humility and gratitude right now?

Allah's Existence
beginner

The Necessity of God's Everlastingness (Al-Baqa')
Sanusi proves God's everlastingness via two reasons: incapacity and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's everlastingness (baqa') is necessary and how the two reasons (incapacity and impossibility) prove that He cannot cease to exist.

Core Idea
God cannot cease to exist because that would make Him possible, leading to origination and absurdity.

After establishing eternity, Sanusi turns to everlastingness: God cannot be receptive to non-existence at any time. He gives two powerful reasons: first, it would entail incapacity and conflict with His cause; second, it would lead to an infinite regress or circularity, rendering His existence impossible. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the conditional: 'if He were receptive to non-existence...'.
The footnote contains the main proof.
2
Identify the two reasons (wajhān)
The passage lists two reasons why God cannot be receptive to non-existence. Find both and note their sub-arguments.
The first reason is about incapacity, the second about impossibility.
3
Study the first reason: incapacity (ajz)
The first reason argues that if God could cease, He would be like other originated things, leading to incapacity, conflict, and arbitrary specification.
Four sub-points are given: equality, tamānu', no specifier, no preference.
4
Study the second reason: impossibility from regress
The second reason shows that if God could cease, He would be possible and need an agent, leading to an infinite chain or circularity, which is impossible.
This mirrors the earlier proof for eternity.
5
Review the concluding note
The note states that baqa' is a necessary consequence of qidam: if eternity is established, non-existence is impossible.
This connects the two attributes.

ويجب له تعالى القدم والبقاء، وإلا كان محتاجاً إلى الفاعل، فيكون حادثاً، فيجب له من العجز ما وجب لسائر الحوادث، بل يكون حينئذ وجوده مستحيلاً؛ لما يلزم على تقدير حدوثه من الدور أو التسلسل المستحيلين. بطلان الدور والتسلسل دليل على قدمه تعالى ثم على بقائه. يجب له تبارك وتعالى أن يكون غير قابل للعدم في الأزل؛ وهو معنى القدم (١)، ولا فيما لا يزال؛ وهو معنى البقاء (٢). يعني: (١) قال العلامة البيلي في تقييداته على شرح صغرى الصغرى (ق ٩): (القدم ذكر بعد الوجود من ذكر اللازم؛ لأن من وجب وجوده ثبت قدمه، والقدم في حق ذاته العلية وصفاته السنية قدم ذات؛ وهو نفي العدم السابق للوجود؛ أي: فوجوده تعالى لا يقبل العدم في الأزل، أو نفي الأولية للوجود؛ إذ القديم الذي لا أول له، فهو الأول، أو نفي افتتاح الوجود، والعبارات الثلاث متقاربة؛ لأن متعلقاتها مختلفة، فقول الهدهدي في «شرح العقيدة الصغرى»: «معناها واحد» أي: يرجع إلى المقصد في تعريف القدم، وليست متحدة مفهوماً وماصدقاً). (٢) قال العلامة البيلي في «تقييداته على شرح صغرى الصغرى» (ق ٩): إذ لو كان قابلاً للعدم لما كان واجب الوجود، بل كان جائز الوجود، وكل جائز الوجود فهو مفتقر إلى الفاعل كسائر الجائزات (١)، فيكون حادثاً مثلها، وذلك (٢) مستحيل لوجهين: أحدهما: أنه يلزم أن يكون عاجزاً كسائر الحوادث؛ المساواته لها في الحدوث والجواز، فلا يصح إسناد شيء من الحوادث إليه؛ لعموم العجز عن الإيجاد لكل حادث، وأيضاً: يلزم عجزه عن الإيجاد من أجل التمانع بينه وبين موجده الذي افتقر إليه، وأيضاً: إسناد الممكنات إليه بالخصوص دون موجده.. تخصيص بلا مخصص، وأيضاً: فليس استناد سائر الممكنات إليه بأولى من العكس. الثاني: أنه يلزم أن يكون وجوده حينئذ مستحيلاً لا يتصور في العقل ثبوته؛ لأنه إن قدر قبول ذاته للعدم صار جائزاً مفتقراً إلى الفاعل، ويلزم أن يكون فاعله جائزاً مفتقراً إلى الفاعل؛ لأنه مثله في الألوهية (٣)، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى فاعل الفاعل، ثم كذلك أبداً. فإن انتهى العدد وانحصر.. لزم الدور، فيلزم أن يكون الأول الذي انتهى إليه العدد إنما أوجده بعض من بعده ممن تأخر وجوده. (عطفه على القدم عطف لازم على ملزوم؛ لأن من ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، والمقصد التنصيص على كل صفة بعينها؛ لأن المقام مقام اعتقادي) (١) لأن ثبوت العلة يستلزم ثبوت المعلول (٢) يعني: جميع ما تقدم؛ من قبول العدم والجواز والافتقار والحدوث (٣) يعني: على سبيل الفرض و
And it is necessary for Him, exalted, eternity and everlastingness. Otherwise, He would be in need of an agent, so He would be originated, and then there would be incumbent on Him from incapacity what is incumbent on other originated things; rather, then His existence would be impossible, due to what follows from the supposition of His origination of circularity or infinite regress, both impossible. The falsity of circularity and infinite regress is a proof for His eternity, exalted, then for His everlastingness. It is necessary for Him, blessed and exalted, that He not be receptive to non-existence in pre-eternity—that is the meaning of eternity (1)—nor in what is everlasting—that is the meaning of everlastingness (2). Meaning: (1) al-Baylī said in his glosses on Sharh al-Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā (folio 9): “Eternity is mentioned after existence as a mention of the necessary consequent, because whoever’s existence is necessary, his eternity is established. Eternity in respect of His exalted essence and His elevated attributes is eternity of essence, and it is the negation of prior non-existence to existence, i.e., His existence, exalted, does not accept non-existence in pre-eternity, or the negation of primacy to existence, for the eternal is that which has no first, so He is the First, or the negation of the beginning of existence. The three expressions are close, because their referents differ. So al-Hudhdī said in Sharḥ al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā: ‘Their meaning is one’ meaning it returns to the intent in defining eternity, but they are not unified in concept and extension.” (2) al-Baylī said in his glosses on Sharḥ al-Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā (folio 9): “For if He were receptive to non-existence, He would not be necessary of existence, but rather would be possible of existence. And every possible of existence is in need of an agent like other possible things (1), so He would be originated like them. And that (2) is impossible for two reasons: The first: that it would entail that He be incapable like other originated things, due to His equality with them in origination and possibility, so it would not be correct to attribute anything of originated things to Him, due to the generality of incapability of bringing into existence for every originated thing. And also, His incapability of bringing into existence would be entailed because of the prevention (tamānuʿ) between Him and the one who brought Him into existence, to whom He is in need. And also, the attribution of possible things to Him specifically rather than to the one who brought Him into existence is a specification without a specifier. And also, the dependence of all other possible things on Him is not more likely than the opposite. The second: that it would entail that His existence then would be impossible, not conceivable in the intellect as established, because if His essence is supposed to accept non-existence, it becomes possible and in need of an agent, and it would be necessary that its agent be possible and in need of an agent, because it is like it in divinity (3). Then we transfer the discussion to the agent of that agent, and so on forever. If the number ends and is limited, circularity would be entailed, and then it would be necessary that the first, at which the number ends, was brought into existence by some of those after it, whose existence is later. (Its conjunction with eternity is a conjunction of a necessary consequence with its necessitating condition, because whoever’s eternity is established, his non-existence is impossible. And the intent is to specify each attribute distinctly, because the context is a context of belief.) (1) Because the establishment of the cause necessitates the establishment of the effect. (2) Meaning: all that preceded—from acceptance of non-existence, possibility, need, and origination. (3) Meaning: by way of assumption. And
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of God's eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa'). The author (Sanusi) begins by stating that eternity and everlastingness are necessary for God, then presents a proof: if God were not eternal, He would need an agent and thus be originated, which would entail incapacity and impossibility. The falsity of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) serves as evidence for His eternity. The passage then defines eternity as being non-receptive to non-existence in pre-eternity, and everlastingness as being non-receptive to non-existence in the future. Two extensive footnotes from al-Baylī clarify that eternity is a necessary consequent of necessary existence, and that the definitions of eternity are varied but converge on the same intent. The main argument then proves everlastingness: if God could cease to exist, He would be possible, thus needing an agent, leading to origination. This is impossible for two reasons. First, it would entail incapacity, as He would be like other originated things, unable to bring about events, and would conflict with His own agent (tamānuʿ). Second, it would lead to an infinite regress or circularity, making His existence impossible. The footnotes comment that the conjunction of everlastingness with eternity is a necessary consequence, and that the two reasons are based on the impossibility of a possible being existing without a cause. The passage concludes with the circularity argument: if a chain of agents ends, the first would be caused by a later one, which is absurd.

بقاء (baqa')
جائز الوجود (ja'iz al-wujud)
عجز (ajz)
تمانع (tamānu')
تخصيص (takhsis)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God were created, He could still be powerful and exist forever.
Correction: Sanusi argues that createdness would entail incapacity and lead to an impossible regress, making God's existence impossible.

B) Incapacity and conflict of will
C) Incomparability and self-sufficiency
D) Incapacity and impossibility from circularity/regress

Key Takeaway
God's everlastingness is necessary because the possibility of His non-existence would lead to incapacity or an impossible regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Sanusi argues that possibility leads to incapacity. In your own life, when you consider something 'possible but not certain', does that uncertainty weaken your commitment? How can you cultivate certainty in God's everlastingness despite life's uncertainties?
?
The proof shows that God's non-existence would require an infinite regress of causes. Are there areas in your faith where you secretly hope for a 'plan B' if God fails you? How can you root out that subtle doubt and fully embrace that He is the end of all chains of reliance?
