Proof that the Necessary Existent Cannot Be Subject to Non-Existence
Sanusi proves with two arguments that the Necessary Existent cannot be subject to non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments that prove the Necessary Existent cannot be subject to non-existence, and why such a possibility leads to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
If God could cease to exist, He wouldn't be God — and nothing could exist at all.

Sanusi now presents a rigorous proof that the Necessary Existent cannot possibly be subject to non-existence. He first establishes that if it were possible, it would be contingent and thus originated. Then he shows two devastating consequences: the first, that it would be incapable like all originated things; the second, that its very existence would become impossible due to circularity or infinite regress. This passage is foundational for understanding the absolute necessity of God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the formal structure: 'If... then... for two reasons: the first... the second...'
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage presents two reasons (وجهان) why the outcome is impossible. Find where the second reason begins and note the contrast between them.
The first reason is about incapacity; the second about impossibility of existence.
3
Work through the first argument: incapacity
The first argument shows that if the Necessary Existent were contingent, it would share the incapacity of all originated things. Look for the four sub-points: 1) general incapacity, 2) mutual prevention, 3) arbitrary specification, 4) no reason for preference.
Consider how each sub-point reinforces the conclusion that a contingent being cannot be the source of all existence.
4
Work through the second argument: impossible existence
The second argument shows that if the Necessary Existent were contingent, its existence would be impossible due to circularity or infinite regress. Understand the two branches: ending in a circle or continuing infinitely. Reflect on why both are absurd.
This argument relies on the principle that an infinite past is impossible; only an infinite future is conceivable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ لو كان قابلاً للعدم لَمَا كانَ واجب الوجودِ ، بل كان جائز الوجودِ ، وكلُّ جائز الوجودِ فهو مُفتقر إلى الفاعل كسائر الجائزاتِ(۱) ، فيكونُ حادثاً مثلها ، وذلك (۲) مستحيل لوجهين : أحدهما : أنَّه يلزم أن يكون عاجزاً كسائر الحوادث ؛ المساواته لها في الحدوثِ والجواز ، فلا يصح إسنادُ شيءٍ مِنَ الحوادثِ إليه ؛ لعموم العجز عن الإيجادِ لكل حادث ، وأيضاً : يلزم عجزُهُ عنِ الإيجادِ مِنْ أجلِ التمانعِ بينَهُ وبينَ موجدِهِ الذي افتقر إليه ، وأيضاً : إسنادُ الممكناتِ إليه بالخصوص دون موجدِهِ . . تخصيص بلا مُخصص، وأيضاً : فليسَ استناد سائر الممكناتِ إليه بأولى مِنَ العكس . الثاني : أنَّهُ يلزم أن يكون وجودُهُ حينئذٍ مستحيلاً لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل ثبوتُه ؛ لأنَّهُ إِنْ قُدِّرَ قَبولُ ذاتِهِ للعدم صارَ جائزاً مُفتقراً إلى الفاعل ، ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ فاعلُهُ جائزاً مُفتقراً إلى الفاعل ؛ لأنَّه مثله في الألوهية (۳) ، ثمَّ ننقلُ الكلامَ إلى فاعل الفاعل ، ثم كذلك أبداً فإن انتهى العدد وانحصر . . لزم الدورُ ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ الأَوَّلُ الذي انتهى إليهِ العددُ إِنَّما أوجدَهُ بعضُ مَنْ بعدَهُ ممَّنْ تأَخَّرَ وجودُهُ عنه ، فيكون سابقاً عليه في الوجودِ متأخراً عنه ، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ . وإنْ لم ينته العدد ، بل تسلسل إلى غيرِ أَوَّل . . لزم وجودُ ما لا نهايةَ له عدداً ، والفراغ مِنْ ذلك فيما مضى ، وذلك لا يُعقَلُ(۱) ؛ إذ ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ الأعدادِ كأنفاس أهل الجنة وأزمنتهم ونعمهم (۲) مثلاً لا يَسعُهُ إلا المستقبلُ ؛ بأن يوجد فيه شيئاً بعد شيءٍ أبد الآبادِ ، وأمَّا أن يوجد كلُّه في الحال أو المضي .. فلا يُعقَلُ . فقولنا : ( بل يكون حينئذٍ وجوده مستحيلاً ) إضراب انتقال مِنْ لازم محال إلى لازم أشدَّ منه في الاستحالة ، لا إضراب إبطال ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
For if He were receptive to non-existence, then He would not be the Necessary Existent, but rather would be a possible existent, and every possible existent is in need of an agent like all other possible things, so He would be originated like them. And that is impossible for two reasons: The first: that He would then be incapable like all other originated things, due to His equal to them in origination and possibility, so it would not be correct to ascribe any originated thing to Him, because of the general incapacity of every originated thing for bringing into existence. Also: His incapacity for bringing into existence would follow due to the mutual prevention between Him and the one who brought Him into existence to whom He is in need. Also: ascribing the possible things to Him specifically rather than to His originator would be a specification without a specifier. Also: the dependence of all other possible things on Him is not more likely than the opposite. The second: that then His existence would be impossible, not conceivable in the intellect that it be established; because if His essence is supposed to be receptive to non-existence, He becomes possible and in need of an agent, and it would follow that His agent is also possible and in need of an agent, because He is like Him in divinity. Then we transfer the discourse to the agent of the agent, and so on forever. If the chain of agents ends and terminates, then circularity follows, because then the first at which the chain ends would be brought into existence by someone after him who is later in existence than him, so that first would be prior to that later in existence yet posterior to him, and that is inconceivable. And if the chain does not end but rather extends infinitely without a first, then the existence of that which has no end in number would follow, and to have completed that in the past is inconceivable; for that which has no end in number, like the breaths of the people of Paradise and their times and blessings for example, cannot be accommodated except by the future, in that it comes into existence one after another forever; but as for it all existing at once in the present or in the past, that is inconceivable. So our saying: 'Rather, then His existence would be impossible' is a transition from one impossible implication to a more severe impossible implication, not an invalidating transition. And with Allah the Exalted is success.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the Necessary Existent (واجب الوجود) cannot be subject to non-existence (العدم). The author presents two main arguments (وجهان). The argument begins by stating that if the Necessary Existent were receptive to non-existence, it would no longer be necessary but contingent (جائز الوجود). Every contingent existent requires an agent, and thus would be originated (حادث) like all other contingents. This leads to two impossibilities. The first impossibility (الوجه الأول) is that the necessary being would then be incapable (عاجز) like other originated things, because it would share in origination and contingency. Several sub-arguments follow: (1) It would be invalid to ascribe any originated act to Him because every originated thing is inherently incapable of creation. (2) There would be mutual prevention (تمانع) between Him and His own originator, making Him incapable. (3) Ascribing all other possibilities to Him rather than to His originator would be arbitrary specification (تخصيص بلا مخصص). (4) There is no reason why all possible things depend on Him rather than on His originator. The second impossibility (الوجه الثاني) is that His existence would then become impossible altogether. If He accepts non-existence, He becomes contingent and requires an agent. That agent would itself be contingent and require another agent, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل) or a circle (دور). If the chain ends at a first, circularity occurs because that first would be created by someone after it, which is contradictory (it would be both prior and posterior). If the chain is infinite without a first, then there would be an infinite number of past events, which is inconceivable because an infinite series cannot be completed in the past; it can only proceed indefinitely into the future. Therefore, the original statement 'rather His existence would be impossible' is a transition from one impossible consequence to an even stronger impossibility. The proof relies on foundational principles of contingency, origination, and the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.

جائز الوجود
العدم
الحدوث
العجز
التمانع
التخصيص بلا مخصص
الدور
التسلسل
الاستحالة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof denies God's power to annihilate creation or that it limits His omnipotence.
Correction: The proof is about God's essence, not His actions. It affirms that God's existence is necessary by nature — He cannot not exist. This does not affect His ability to create or destroy contingent things.

Checkpoint
What two main impossibilities does Sanusi argue follow if the Necessary Existent were subject to non-existence?
A) That He would be both eternal and temporal; that He would be impossible.
B) That He would be incapable like all originated things; that His existence would be impossible.
C) That He would be powerless; that He would be non-existent.
D) That He would be contingent; that He would be originated.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Existent's impossibility of non-existence is proven by showing that its possibility would reduce it to a contingent being, leading to logical contradictions: either incapacity or the impossibility of existence itself.

Are you willing to let them go in your heart, and instead anchor your security only in Him? What is one attachment you will surrender today?
?
This proof shows that if Allah could cease to exist, nothing could exist at all. When you feel your faith weakening, do you realize that you are essentially doubting the very foundation of existence? Proof of the Impossibility of Infinite Regress
Sanusi proves the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite regress of causes is logically impossible according to Sanusi's argument.

Core Idea
An infinite series cannot be completed in the past, only in the future.

Sanusi presents a rigorous proof that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. He considers two possibilities: either the chain of causes terminates at a first cause or it does not. Both lead to logical absurdities. This lesson will walk through his reasoning and the key concept of past versus future infinities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the two conditional statements: 'إذا تناهى العدد...' and 'وإن لم ينته العدد...'
2
Identify the two hypothetical cases
The passage presents two scenarios: one where the chain of causes terminates, and one where it does not (infinite regress). Identify each case and note the absurdity that follows.
First case: the cause would be both prior and posterior. Second case: an infinite number would be completed in the past.
3
Understand the absurdity in each case
For the first case, the cause would be prior to itself, which is inconceivable. For the second case, completing an infinite series in the past is impossible. Reflect on why each is logically untenable.
The second case is the core of the proof against infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the example from Paradise
Sanusi illustrates with the breaths and blessings of Paradise: an infinite series can only be realized sequentially in the future, not all at once in the past or present.
This example clarifies why an infinite past is impossible.
5
Consider the rhetorical shift at the end
The final remark clarifies that the statement about impossibility is a transition to a stronger impossibility, not a refutation of the previous point.
This is a technical point about argumentative style.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنه ، فيكون سابقاً عليه في الوجودِ متأخراً عنه ، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ . وإنْ لم ينته العدد ، بل تسلسل إلى غيرِ أَوَّل . . لزم وجودُ ما لا نهايةَ له عدداً ، والفراغ مِنْ ذلك فيما مضى ، وذلك لا يُعقَلُ(۱) ؛ إذ ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ الأعدادِ كأنفاس أهل الجنة وأزمنتهم ونعمهم (۲) مثلاً لا يَسعُهُ إلا المستقبلُ ؛ بأن يوجد فيه شيئاً بعد شيءٍ أبد الآبادِ ، وأمَّا أن يوجد كلُّه في الحال أو المضي .. فلا يُعقَلُ . فقولنا : ( بل يكون حينئذٍ وجوده مستحيلاً ) إضراب انتقال مِنْ لازم محال إلى لازم أشدَّ منه في الاستحالة ، لا إضراب إبطال ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
(۱) إذ الفراغ من ذلك فيما مضى دليل النهاية ، وهو مخالف لمفهوم التسلسل الذي معناه : عدم الفراغ ؛ فالاستحالة للجمع بين الفراغ ونقيضه عدم الفراغ ؛ كدورات القمر إلى اليوم مثلاً منتهية بهذا اليوم
(۲) في (د ، و ) : ( ونعيمهم ) .
From it, so it would be prior to it in existence and posterior to it, and that is inconceivable. And if the number does not terminate, but rather proceeds in infinite regress, the existence of something infinite in number follows, and its completion in the past, and that is inconceivable(1); for that which is infinite in number—like the breaths of the people of Paradise, their times, and their blessings(2)—can only be contained by the future, by existing in it one after another forever. As for its existing all at once in the present or the past, that is inconceivable. Our statement: '(Rather, its existence is then impossible)' is a transitional disjunction from a necessary impossible to a more severe impossibility, not a disjunction of nullification. And success is from Allah, the Exalted.
(1) For completion in the past is evidence of an end, which contradicts the concept of infinite regress, which means non-completion; so the impossibility is due to combining completion and its opposite, non-completion—like the revolutions of the moon up to today, for example, ending with this day.
(2) In (D, W): 'and their bliss.'
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the impossibility of an infinite regress (تسلسل). It first considers a chain of causes: if the chain terminates, the last cause would be prior and posterior to another, which is absurd. If the chain does not terminate, an infinite number of past events would have to be completed, which is also absurd. The author illustrates this with the example of the breaths and blessings of Paradise: an infinite series can only be realized sequentially in the future, never all at once in the past or present. The final remark clarifies that the statement 'بل يكون حينئذٍ وجوده مستحيلاً' is a rhetorical shift to a stronger impossibility, not a refutation of the previous point. The footnotes further explain the logical contradiction between completion and infinite regress, and note a textual variant.

Key Terms
تسلسل
لا نهاية
إضراب انتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite series of past events can be completed if given infinite time.
Correction: The passage argues that completing an infinite series in the past is impossible; only the future can contain an infinite succession.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider an infinite regress of causes impossible?
A) Because the number of causes would be finite.
B) Because it would require completing an infinite series in the past, which is inconceivable.
C) Because Allah cannot create an infinite number of things.
D) Because the Quran explicitly denies it.

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress of causes is impossible because it would require completing an infinite past, which is logically inconceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The lesson uses the example of the breaths and blessings of Paradise: an infinite series only possible in the future, not the past. Think of your own sins and shortcomings—are you trying to 'complete' an infinite account of your past mistakes through regret or self-punishment? How does the logical truth about infinity free you to accept Allah's mercy sequentially in the future, rather than being trapped in an impossible desire to undo the infinite past?

How does the impossibility of infinite regress call you to cut that chain and face the First Cause of your actions—your own choices or Allah's decree? What concrete step will you take today to stop deferring and acknowledge the ultimate cause?

Refuting the Premise of Corporeality and Affirming Divine Uniqueness
Sanusi refutes the premise that any essence with attributes is a body, and concludes God's utter uniqueness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the second false premise (that any essence with attributes is a body) and concludes that God is utterly unique and incomparable.

Core Idea
Having attributes does not make God a body; He is unique in essence.

Sanusi continues his proof of God's self-subsistence by addressing a common misunderstanding: the assumption that any entity with attributes must be a body. He shows that God's attributes do not imply corporeality, and then draws a powerful conclusion about God's absolute uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow from refutation to conclusion.
Pay attention to the two 'parts' mentioned: al-juz' al-awwal and al-juz' al-thani.
2
Identify the two components of the refutation
The passage refutes the second premise (that every essence with attributes is a body). Find how Sanusi dismantles this illusion and then distinguishes God from attributes and from contingent essences.
The first part distinguishes God from attributes; the second distinguishes Him from all essences.
3
Connect to the creedal formula
Read the final line which summarizes the creed: God is an essence qualified with attributes, independent of a substrate and independent of an agent. How does this encapsulate the argument?
This formula is the foundation of the Islamic belief in God's uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجزء الثاني أبطل المقدمة الثانية؛ وهي اعتقاد الوهم: أن كل ذات موصوفة بالصفات فهي جرم؛ فإن مولانا جل وعلا موصوف بالصفات، وهو مع ذلك يستحيل أن يكون جرماً أو مماثلاً لشيء من الحوادث، فهو تعالى ذات حقيقة، ولا مثل له من الذوات، وبهذا الجزء باينت ذاته تعالى سائر الذوات الحادثة؛ فإنها وإن كانت غنية عن المحل - أي: لا تكون صفة قائمة بغيرها - فهي مفتقرة إلى الفاعل افتقاراً لازماً لا يمكن انفكاكها عنه ضرورة؛ لوجوب حدوثها، وافتقارها إلى المولى الكريم جل وعلا ابتداءً ودواماً. والحاصل: أن بالجزء الأول من معنى القيام بالنفس باين جل وعلا الصفات؛ فليس من جنسها، وبالجزء الثاني باين تبارك وتعالى سائر الذوات؛ فلا شبيه له منها، ولا يشاركها في أجناسها ولا فصولها ولا خواصها.
فقولنا في أصل العقيدة: (أي: ذاتاً موصوفاً بالصفات، غنياً عن المحل وغنيّاً عن الفاعل).
And the second part refuted the second premise—which is the illusion's belief that every essence qualified with attributes is a body. For our Lord, mighty and majestic, is qualified with attributes, and yet it is impossible that He be a body or similar to anything among contingent things. He, exalted, is a true Essence, and there is nothing like Him among essences. By this part, His essence, exalted, is distinguished from all contingent essences. For although they are independent of a substrate—i.e., they are not an attribute subsisting in another—they are dependent upon an agent with a necessary dependence that cannot be separated from them of necessity, due to the necessitation of their origination and their dependence on the generous Lord, exalted, from the beginning and perpetually. The conclusion: by the first part of the meaning of self-subsistence, He, mighty and majestic, is distinguished from attributes; so He is not of their genus. And by the second part, He, blessed and exalted, is distinguished from all other essences; so there is no likeness to Him from among them, nor does He share with them in their genera, their differentiae, or their properties.
Thus our statement in the foundation of belief: 'i.e., an essence qualified with attributes, independent of a substrate and independent of an agent.'
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the second refutation and a final conclusion. The second false premise claimed that any essence possessing attributes must be a body. Sanusi refutes it: God has attributes but is not a body nor similar to any contingent being. Using the second component of self-subsistence (independence from an agent), he shows that God's necessary existence distinguishes Him from contingent essences. Contingent beings, though independent of a substrate (they are not attributes), are necessarily dependent on an agent for their existence. Thus, by the first part, God is not an attribute; by the second, He is not a body or any contingent being. The creedal statement summarizes: God is an essence with attributes, independent of a substrate and independent of an agent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any essence that possesses attributes must be a body (jirm).
Correction: God possesses attributes, yet He is not a body and is not similar to any contingent being. Attributes do not entail corporeality.

Proof That God Is Not an Attribute Using Infinite Regress
Sanusi proves God is not an attribute via infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be an attribute and how the argument of infinite regress demonstrates this.

Core Idea
If God were an attribute, describing Him would lead to an infinite chain of attributes.

Sanusi now provides the rational proof that God is not an attribute. He argues that if God were an attribute, He would have to be describable by other attributes, which would lead to an infinite regress of attributes upon attributes. This argument establishes the first part of self-subsistence: that God is an essence, not an attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation; notice how Sanusi structures the proof.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The key is: 'if God were an attribute, then He could not be described by attributes.' Understand why that is problematic.
3
Understand the infinite regress
The proof explains that if an attribute could accept description, then every attribute would need another attribute ad infinitum, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا برهانُ الجزء الأول - وهو أنَّهُ تعالى ذات لا صفة - : فما أشرنا إليه في أصل العقيدة ؛ وهو أنَّه جلَّ وعلا لو كان صفةً لزمَ ألا يتصفَ بالصفاتِ الوجودية ؛ وهي صفات المعاني التي هي القدرة والإرادة والعلم والحياة ... إلى آخرِها ، ولا بلوازمها التي هي المعنوية ؛ وهي كونه تعالى قادراً ومريداً وعالماً وحياً... إلى آخرِها ، والدليلُ القطعي دلَّ على وجوبِ اتصافه تعالى بها ، فليس بصفةٍ ؛ لأنَّ الصفة لو قبلَتْ أنْ تتَّصف بالصفاتِ الوجودية . . لاستحال عُرُو كلِّ صفةٍ عنها كما في الذواتِ (١) ؛ لأنَّ القَبول نفسي ، فلا يتخلَّفُ ؛ وذلك يستلزم التسلسل ، ودخول ما لا نهاية له في الوجودِ ؛ لأنَّه يجب لصفة الصفة ما وجبَ للصفة الأولى مِنَ الاتصاف بالصفاتِ الوجودية ، ثمَّ هكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له ، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ)
(١) العُرُو هنا : الخلو، يقال : فلان عِرْو من الأمر؛ أي : خِلْو منه، وسببه هنا : أن المحل القابل للشيء لا يخلو عنه، أو مثله، أو عن ضده
As for the proof of the first part — which is that He (Exalted) is an essence not an attribute — it is what we indicated in the foundational creed: that He (Exalted and Majestic) if He were an attribute, it would necessarily follow that He would not be described by existential attributes, which are the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) which are power, will, knowledge, life ... etc., nor by their concomitants which are the moral attributes (sifat al-ma'nawiyya); which are His being Powerful, Willing, Knowing, Living ... etc. And the decisive proof has indicated the obligation of His being described by them, so He is not an attribute; because if an attribute were to accept being described by existential attributes, it would be impossible for any attribute to be devoid of them, as is the case in essences (1); because the acceptance is essential, so it does not fail; and that entails infinite regress (tasalsul), and the entry of what has no end into existence; because what is necessary for the attribute of the attribute is what is necessary for the first attribute in terms of being described by existential attributes, then so on ad infinitum, and that is not intelligible. (1) al-'uru here means emptiness; it is said: so-and-so is empty of the matter; i.e., devoid of it. And its cause here: that the locus which is receptive to a thing is not devoid of it, or its like, or its opposite.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the first part of self-subsistence, i.e., that God is not an attribute. The argument is: if God were an attribute, He could not be described by existential attributes like power, will, etc., and their concomitants (being powerful, etc.), but decisive proof shows He must be described by them. Therefore He is not an attribute. The reason: if an attribute could accept being described by existential attributes, then no attribute could be devoid of them, because acceptance is essential (cannot fail). This would lead to infinite regress, which is impossible. The footnote explains the term 'عُرُو' meaning emptiness or being devoid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an attribute could itself be described by other attributes without leading to a problem.
Correction: The proof shows that if an attribute could accept being described, its essential acceptance would force every attribute to be described, leading to an infinite regress of attributes, which is rationally impossible.

The Infinite Regress Proof for God's Independence
Al-Baylī's infinite regress argument for God's independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of infinite regress proves that God cannot have a creator and is therefore absolutely independent.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible; therefore God needs no originator.

Sanusi states that the second proof is self-evident, but al-Baylī unpacks it with a logical argument: if God needed a creator, that creator would need one, and so on, leading to an impossible infinite chain. This lesson walks through that reasoning and why it establishes God's absolute independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the main text and al-Baylī's clarification
Start by reading Sanusi's brief line and then al-Baylī's detailed gloss. Notice how the proof is presented as a conditional argument.
Al-Baylī's text contains the full logical structure.
2
Understand the conditional reasoning
The argument: If God needed an originator, then that originator would need another, leading to an infinite regress. Since infinite regress is impossible, the original assumption is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Apply the reasoning to the conclusion
The conclusion is that God is independent of any agent (fā‘il). Reflect on why this proof is considered clear by Sanusi.
The proof relies on the impossibility of tasalsul (infinite chain).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما برهان الجزء الثاني : فواضح لا يحتاج إلى بيان (۱) ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق (٢)
(۱) قال العلامة البيلي في « تقييداته على شرح صغرى الصغرى » ( ق ۱۱ ) : ( توضيح برهان الجزء الثاني : أن تقول : لو كان محتاجاً إلى محدث يوجده لاحتاج محدثه إلى محدث ، ومحدث محدثه إلى محدث وهكذا ، فيلزم المحال السابق ، وما أدى إلى المحال محال ، فيثبت غَناؤه عن الفاعل ، وهو المطلوب )
(۲) قال العلامة البيلي في " تقييداته على شرح صغرى الصغرى » ( ق ۱۱ ) : ) واعلم : أن الأشياء بالنسبة للمحلَّ والفاعل على أربعة أقسام : أحدها : ما لا يحتاج إليهما ؛ وهي ذات الله عز وجل ، وثانيها : ما يحتاج إليهما معاً صفات الحوادث ، وثالثها : ما يحتاج إلى محل ولا يحتاج إلى فاعل ؛ كصفاته جل وعز ، ورابعها : ما يحتاج إلى فاعل دون محل ؛ كذوات وهي الحوادث ) .
As for the proof of the second part: it is clear and needs no explanation (1). And with Allah the Exalted is all success (2). (1) The scholar al-Baylī said in his glosses on Sharḥ Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā (folio 11): (Clarification of the proof of the second part: that you say: If He were in need of an originator to bring Him into existence, then His originator would be in need of an originator, and the originator of His originator would be in need of an originator, and so on. Thus the previously mentioned impossibility would follow, and whatever leads to impossibility is impossible. Thus His independence from an agent is proven, and that is the desired conclusion.) (2) The scholar al-Baylī said in his glosses on Sharḥ Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā (folio 11): (And know: things relative to substrate and agent are of four divisions: First: what is in need of neither of the two; that is the essence of Allah, Mighty and Majestic. Second: what is in need of both together; that is the attributes of originated events. Third: what is in need of a substrate but not in need of an agent; like His attributes, Glorious and Exalted. Fourth: what is in need of an agent without a substrate; that is the essences of originated events.)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (burhān al-juz’ al-thānī) that God is independent and in no need of a creator. The main text states that this proof is clear and requires no elaboration, but the commentator al-Baylī (d. ?) provides a clarification. The proof is a classic argument from infinite regress: if God were in need of an originator (muḥdith) to bring Him into existence, then that originator would also need an originator, and so on ad infinitum. This infinite chain is impossible (muḥāl), hence the antecedent (God needing an originator) is false. Therefore, God is independent of any agent (fā‘il) – which is the desired conclusion. Al-Baylī then adds a quadruple division of all things relative to substrate (maḥall) and agent (fā‘il): (1) that which needs neither – God’s essence; (2) that which needs both – the attributes of originated events (i.e., accidental qualities); (3) that which needs a substrate but not an agent – God’s attributes; (4) that which needs an agent but not a substrate – the essences (dhawāt) of originated events (i.e., contingent substances). This classification further clarifies the unique status of God’s essence and attributes.

Key Terms
برهان
غناء
فاعل
محدث
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is an immediate self-evident statement without need for reasoning.
Correction: The proof actually requires a logical argument based on the impossibility of infinite regress, as al-Baylī clarifies.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what would happen if God were in need of an originator?
A) He would not be God.
B) An infinite regress of originators would arise, which is impossible.
C) The originator would be identical to God.
D) The proof would be invalid.

Key Takeaway
God's independence from a creator is proven by the impossibility of infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God needs no one and nothing, why do you exhaust yourself seeking approval from people who themselves are needy? What would it look like to anchor your worth in His independence rather than human validation?
?
When you panic about losing a relationship, a job, or your health, does the truth that God alone is the Self-Sufficient Originator ever cross your mind? How can you train your heart to turn to Him first in those moments?
?
The proof says infinite regress is impossible and God must be independent. But do you live as if you believe that—or do you act as if you need a thousand things to be okay? What is one created thing you can surrender today to remind yourself that He is enough?

The Fourfold Classification of Beings
Al-Baylī's fourfold classification of beings relative to substrate and agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of beings based on their need for a substrate and an agent, and where God's essence and attributes fit.

Core Idea
Everything in existence falls into one of four categories regarding need for substrate and agent; only God's essence needs neither.

After establishing God's independence, al-Baylī provides a comprehensive classification of all things based on their relationship to substrate (maḥall) and agent (fā‘il). This framework clarifies the unique status of God's essence and attributes, and the nature of contingent things.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Baylī's classification passage
Carefully read the Arabic and translation of the four categories. Notice how each is defined by the need (or lack thereof) for maḥall and fā‘il.
The classification is exhaustive: all things can be placed in one of these four.
2
Understand each category individually
Go through the four categories one by one: (1) needs neither, (2) needs both, (3) needs substrate only, (4) needs agent only. Reflect on the examples given.
Pay attention to the difference between God's essence (no need) and His attributes (need substrate).
3
Apply by identifying examples
Try to think of other examples beyond those given, such as accidental qualities (e.g., color) needing both, or substances like atoms needing an agent but not a substrate.
This classification is foundational in kalam ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما برهان الجزء الثاني : فواضح لا يحتاج إلى بيان (۱) ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق (٢)
(۱) قال العلامة البيلي في « تقييداته على شرح صغرى الصغرى » ( ق ۱۱ ) : ( توضيح برهان الجزء الثاني : أن تقول : لو كان محتاجاً إلى محدث يوجده لاحتاج محدثه إلى محدث ، ومحدث محدثه إلى محدث وهكذا ، فيلزم المحال السابق ، وما أدى إلى المحال محال ، فيثبت غَناؤه عن الفاعل ، وهو المطلوب )
(۲) قال العلامة البيلي في " تقييداته على شرح صغرى الصغرى » ( ق ۱۱ ) : ) واعلم : أن الأشياء بالنسبة للمحلَّ والفاعل على أربعة أقسام : أحدها : ما لا يحتاج إليهما ؛ وهي ذات الله عز وجل ، وثانيها : ما يحتاج إليهما معاً صفات الحوادث ، وثالثها : ما يحتاج إلى محل ولا يحتاج إلى فاعل ؛ كصفاته جل وعز ، ورابعها : ما يحتاج إلى فاعل دون محل ؛ كذوات وهي الحوادث ) .
As for the proof of the second part: it is clear and needs no explanation (1). And with Allah the Exalted is all success (2). (1) The scholar al-Baylī said in his glosses on Sharḥ Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā (folio 11): (Clarification of the proof of the second part: that you say: If He were in need of an originator to bring Him into existence, then His originator would be in need of an originator, and the originator of His originator would be in need of an originator, and so on. Thus the previously mentioned impossibility would follow, and whatever leads to impossibility is impossible. Thus His independence from an agent is proven, and that is the desired conclusion.) (2) The scholar al-Baylī said in his glosses on Sharḥ Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā (folio 11): (And know: things relative to substrate and agent are of four divisions: First: what is in need of neither of the two; that is the essence of Allah, Mighty and Majestic. Second: what is in need of both together; that is the attributes of originated events. Third: what is in need of a substrate but not in need of an agent; like His attributes, Glorious and Exalted. Fourth: what is in need of an agent without a substrate; that is the essences of originated events.)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (burhān al-juz’ al-thānī) that God is independent and in no need of a creator. The main text states that this proof is clear and requires no elaboration, but the commentator al-Baylī (d. ?) provides a clarification. The proof is a classic argument from infinite regress: if God were in need of an originator (muḥdith) to bring Him into existence, then that originator would also need an originator, and so on ad infinitum. This infinite chain is impossible (muḥāl), hence the antecedent (God needing an originator) is false. Therefore, God is independent of any agent (fā‘il) – which is the desired conclusion. Al-Baylī then adds a quadruple division of all things relative to substrate (maḥall) and agent (fā‘il): (1) that which needs neither – God’s essence; (2) that which needs both – the attributes of originated events (i.e., accidental qualities); (3) that which needs a substrate but not an agent – God’s attributes; (4) that which needs an agent but not a substrate – the essences (dhawāt) of originated events (i.e., contingent substances). This classification further clarifies the unique status of God’s essence and attributes.

Key Terms
محل
فاعل
ذات
صفة
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes are completely independent like His essence.
Correction: God's attributes need a substrate (His essence) but not an agent, whereas His essence needs neither.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baylī's classification, which category does God's essence belong to?
A) Needs both substrate and agent
B) Needs substrate but not agent
C) Needs agent but not substrate
D) Needs neither substrate nor agent

Key Takeaway
God's essence is unique in needing no substrate or agent; His attributes need a substrate but no agent; originated things need either both or just an agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's essence needs nothing—no substrate, no agent—do you truly live as if He is completely independent of you? Or do you sometimes act as if your worship adds something to Him? Commit today to acknowledging His absolute self-sufficiency in your prayers and daily life.
?
Your own attributes—like knowledge, strength, anger—exist in you and were brought into being by God. How does that change the way you view your own qualities? Will you humbly attribute every good trait you have to Him, rather than taking pride in them?
?
You are an essence (dhāt) brought into existence by God alone, not inhering in anything else. How does this truth affect your sense of identity? Are you living as if you are self-sufficient or as if your existence is entirely dependent on Him? Make a concrete plan to remind yourself daily that you exist only through His creative act.

Proof of God's Self-Sufficiency (Reductio ad Absurdum)
Sanusi states the proof is clear; al-Bayli explains the reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of God's self-sufficiency (al-ghina) uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to show that God cannot need a creator.

Core Idea
If God needed a creator, an impossible infinite chain would follow.

Having established God's existence, the second part of the argument proves that God does not need any creator. The main text simply states that the proof is clear, but the gloss by al-Bayli unpacks the logical reasoning: a regress of creators leads to impossibility, so God must be self-sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the main text and gloss
Read the Arabic main text: 'وأما برهان الجزء الثاني : فواضح لا يحتاج إلى بيان'. Then read al-Bayli's gloss that explains the reasoning.
The main text is terse; the gloss provides the detailed argument.
2
Follow the reductio logic
Notice the structure: assume God needs a creator → that creator would need a creator → infinite regress → impossible → therefore the original assumption is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Connect to the larger argument
Understand that this proof establishes God's self-sufficiency, which is a key attribute in Islamic theology. The impossibility of an infinite chain of creators shows that the First Cause must be uncaused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما برهان الجزء الثاني : فواضح لا يحتاج إلى بيان (۱) ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق (٢) (۱) قال العلامة البيلي في « تقييداته على شرح صغرى الصغرى » ( ق ۱۱ ) : ( توضيح برهان الجزء الثاني : أن تقول : لو كان محتاجاً إلى محدث يوجده لاحتاج محدثه إلى محدث ، ومحدث محدثه إلى محدث وهكذا ، فيلزم المحال السابق ، وما أدى إلى المحال محال ، فيثبت غَناؤه عن الفاعل ، وهو المطلوب )
As for the proof of the second part: it is clear and needs no explanation (1). And with God Almighty is success (2). (1) The scholar al-Bayli said in his annotations on Sharh Sughra al-Sughra (folio 11): (Clarification of the proof of the second part: that you say: If He were in need of a creator to bring Him into existence, then His creator would be in need of a creator, and the creator of His creator would be in need of a creator, and so on. This leads to the aforementioned impossibility, and what leads to impossibility is impossible. Thus His self-sufficiency from the agent is established, and that is what is sought.)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents and expounds the proof of God's self-sufficiency (al-ghina) from the second part of the argument. The main text states that the proof is clear. The gloss by al-Bayli explains the proof in detail: if God needed a creator (muhdith) to bring Him into existence, that creator would itself need a creator, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible (muhal). Since the impossible is impossible, God's independence from any efficient cause is established. The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: the initial supposition leads to an impossibility, so the supposition is false. Thus God is necessarily self-sufficient, not needing any creator.

Key Terms

غَناؤه عن الفاعل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's self-sufficiency means He could have been created by another, but the proof shows that the very concept of a creator for God leads to absurdity.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that needing a creator leads to an infinite chain, which is impossible, so God cannot have a creator.

Checkpoint

A) He would be dependent on that creator forever.
B) Infinite regress leading to impossibility.
C) That creator would be self-sufficient.
D) It would be possible but unlikely.

Key Takeaway
God's self-sufficiency is proven by the impossibility of an infinite regress of creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider an area of your life where you act as if something other than God is necessary for your well-being. How does the impossibility of an infinite regress expose the emptiness of that dependence and call you to rely solely on the Self-Sufficient?

Why Two Equal Gods Are Impossible
Sanusi proves that two equal gods are impossible via a reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument against the possibility of two equal eternal beings (gods) and why the distinction between them would force each to be contingent.

Core Idea
Two equal gods can't both be eternal; their separation requires a created attribute.

Sanusi now addresses a crucial question: could there be more than one god equal in divinity? Using pure reason, he shows that such a scenario leads to a contradiction. The proof demonstrates that any distinction between two supposed gods would have to be by a contingent (created) attribute—making each god dependent and thus not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice how Sanusi structures his argument: he states the impossibility claim, then gives the proof step by step.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence that states the proof's conclusion: that two equal gods are impossible because each would have to be both necessary and contingent (created). The proof shows this leads to absurdity.
3
Trace the proof's logic
Break down the reasoning: two equals must be distinct. Their distinction cannot be by essential attributes (shared), so it must be by a contingent accidental attribute. Contingent attributes are created. Each god is inseparable from its distinguishing attribute, so each god would be created—contradicting their eternity.
Focus on why the distinguishing attribute must be contingent.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is important for Islamic theology: it affirms tawhid (oneness) and refutes polytheism using rational proofs.
This argument is part of a larger framework of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا شكّ أنَّ وجودَ المثلِ لهُ تعالى يستلزم أن يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ المثلين واجباً (٢) . حادثاً جائزاً (۱) ، ويمنعُ أنْ يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما قديماً وبرهانُ ذلكَ : أنَّ المثلين لما استحالَ أنْ يكونَ أحدهما عينَ الآخَرِ .. لزمَ أن يمتاز أحدهما عنِ الآخَرِ ، وتَميُّزُهُ لا يمكنُ أنْ يكونَ بالذاتيّاتِ الواجبات (۳) ; لوجوب اشتراكِ المثلين في جميعها ، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ بعَرَضي جائز (٤) اختص بهِ أحدهما عن الآخرِ معَ جواز أنْ يكونَ لصاحبه ( ٥ ) ; إذ كلُّ ما اتَّصفَ بهِ أحد المثلينِ مِنَ الجائزاتِ .. فإنَّهُ يجوز أن يَتَّصِفَ  ِبهِ مماثله ، وكلُّ جائز فوجوده لا يكون إلا حادثاً ، فتعيَّن أن يكونَ العَرَضيُّ الذي امتاز بهِ كلٌّ مِنَ المثلين عن الآخرِ حادثاً ، وكلٌّ مِنَ المثلين ملازم لهذا العَرَضي الذي يُميزه عن صاحبه ، فتَعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ هو أيضاً حادثاً ; لأنَّ ملازم الحادث حادث
There is no doubt that the existence of a like (mithl) for Him, Exalted, necessitates that each one of the two equals (mithlayn) be necessary (wājib) ... contingent (jā'iz) occurring (ḥādith), and it prevents that each one of them be eternal (qadīm). And the proof of that: since it is impossible for one of the two equals to be identical to the other, it is necessary that one be distinguished from the other, and its distinction cannot be by the essential necessary attributes (al-dhātiyyāt al-wājibāt) because the two equals must share all of them, so it is determined that it be by a contingent accidental attribute ('araḍ jā'iz) that one of them is distinguished from the other, with the possibility that it be for its counterpart; for every contingent attribute that one of the two equals is described with, it is possible that its like be described with it, and every contingent thing's existence is only occurring (ḥādith), so it is determined that the accidental attribute by which each of the two equals is distinguished from the other is occurring, and each of the two equals is inseparable from this accidental attribute that distinguishes it from its counterpart, so it is determined that it too is occurring, because the inseparable companion of a occurring thing is occurring.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a proof by reductio ad absurdum. Sanusi argues that if there were two equal gods (mithlayn), each would need to be distinct from the other. This distinction cannot be by essential necessary attributes because they share all of them. So it must be by a contingent accidental attribute ('araḍ jā'iz). But contingent attributes are created (ḥādith). Since each god is inseparable from this attribute, each god would also be created, contradicting their being eternal. Hence two eternal gods are impossible.

Key Terms
مثلين
ذاتيات واجبات
عرض جائز
حادث
ملازم الحادث حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God could have a contingent attribute without being created, because attributes are not independent entities.
Correction: Sanusi argues that if the attribute is inseparable from the essence, then the essence shares its temporal origin. The principle 'the inseparable companion of a created thing is created' applies to the essence itself.

Checkpoint
Why cannot two equal gods both be eternal according to Sanusi?
A) Because they would conflict
B) Because they would need a distinguishing contingent attribute which is created
C) Because they would share all attributes
D) Because they would be identical

Key Takeaway
Two equal eternal gods are impossible because any distinction between them would require a created attribute, making each god created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you say 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh', do you truly feel in your heart that nothing else deserves your ultimate trust and fear? What competing 'gods'—money, status, relationships—do you cling to as if they were eternal?

Sanusi's proof shows that any distinction between equals forces creation. If you believe Allah is absolutely unique, why do you still treat some people or idols as if they hold independent power over your life? What will you concretely change today to tear down those false gods?

Proof that Two Co-eternal Gods Are Impossible
Sanusi proves impossibility of two co-eternal gods by arguing distinction requires a contingent attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that two co-eternal gods are impossible, based on the necessity of distinction and the nature of contingent attributes.

Core Idea
Two eternals cannot be distinguished without one being originated, which contradicts divinity.

Sanusi presents a rigorous rational proof against the possibility of two co-eternal gods. The argument hinges on the fact that if two entities are truly similar in all essential attributes, any distinction between them must come from a contingent accidental attribute—and such an attribute is originated, making the entities themselves originated. This lesson walks through each step of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to understand the overall flow of the argument. Note that this is a proof against the possibility of two eternal gods.
The passage begins by rejecting the idea of two co-eternal similar beings.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify the main claims: 1) Two co-eternals cannot be identical, so they must be distinguished. 2) Distinction cannot be by essential attributes because they share all of them. 3) Distinction must be by a contingent accidental attribute. 4) That attribute is originated (hadith). 5) The entity inseparable from an originated attribute is also originated. 6) Origination contradicts divinity.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning step by step. Understand why each conclusion necessarily follows from the previous. Pay special attention to the concept of 'inseparable of an originated thing is originated' (ملازم الحادث حادث).
This is a classic kalam argument using the principle of inseparability.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final conclusion: Since each of the two would be originated, they cannot be divine. Therefore, two co-eternal gods are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثلين واجباً . • حادثاً جائزاً ، ويمنعُ أنْ يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما قديماً وبرهانُ ذلكَ : أنَّ المثلين لما استحالَ أنْ يكونَ أحدهما عينَ الآخَرِ .. لزمَ أن يمتاز أحدهما عنِ الآخَرِ ، وتَميُّزُهُ لا يمكنُ أنْ يكونَ بالذاتيّاتِ الواجبات ؛ لوجوب اشتراكِ المثلين في جميعها ، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ بعَرَضي جائز اختص بهِ أحدهما عن الآخرِ معَ جواز أنْ يكونَ لصاحبه ؛ إذ كلُّ ما اتَّصفَ بهِ أحد المثلينِ مِنَ الجائزاتِ .. فإنَّهُ يجوز أن يَتَّصِفَ  ِبهِ مماثله ، وكلُّ جائز فوجوده لا يكون إلا حادثاً ، فتعيَّن أن يكونَ العَرَضيُّ الذي امتاز بهِ كلٌّ مِنَ المثلين عن الآخرِ حادثاً ، وكلٌّ مِنَ المثلين ملازم لهذا العَرَضي الذي يُميزه عن صاحبه ، فتَعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ هو أيضاً حادثاً ؛ لأنَّ ملازم الحادث حادث . والحدوث ينافي الألوهية ؛ لِمَا عرفت في برهان قدم الإله وبقائه.
The two similars (al-mithlayn) are wajib [both eternal]? No, it is impossible for each of them to be eternal. The proof of that: since it is impossible for one of the two similars to be the very same as the other, it follows that one must be distinguished from the other, and its distinction cannot be by the essential necessary attributes (al-dhatiyyat al-wajibat) because the two similars must share all of them. So it is determined that it be by a contingent accidental attribute (aradi jaiz) which one of them is distinguished by from the other, with the possibility that it could belong to its counterpart. For everything that one of the two similars is described with from among contingent things (al-jaizat), it is possible that its similitude is also described with it. Every contingent thing exists only as originated (hadith). So it is determined that the accidental attribute by which each of the two similars is distinguished from the other is originated, and each of the two similars is inseparable from this accidental attribute that distinguishes it from its counterpart, so it is determined that it also is originated, because the inseparable of an originated thing is originated. And origination (al-huduth) contradicts divinity (al-uluhiyya), as you have known from the proof of the eternity and permanence of God.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the impossibility of two co-eternal gods. The author presents an argument based on the principle of distinction. If there were two similar entities (both eternal), they cannot be identical; they must be distinguished. But they cannot be distinguished by their essential necessary attributes because, by definition, they share all those attributes. Therefore, distinction must be by a contingent accidental attribute that one has and the other lacks. However, any contingent attribute is originated (hadith), and whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is also originated. Thus, each of the two entities would be originated, which contradicts their supposed divinity, since God must be eternal. The conclusion is that two co-eternal gods are impossible.

الذاتيّات الواجبات
العَرَضي الجائز
الجائزات
الحدوث
الألوهية
ملازم الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that two co-eternal gods could be distinguished by their essential attributes (like knowledge or power), each having a unique essential attribute.
Correction: The author shows that if they are truly similar (al-mithlayn), they must share all essential necessary attributes. Therefore, distinction cannot be by essential attributes; it must be by a contingent accidental attribute.

Checkpoint

B) Because the two similars must share all essential necessary attributes.
C) Because essential attributes are contingent.
D) Because distinction requires an attribute that one lacks.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two co-eternal gods is proven by the necessity of distinction through a contingent, originated attribute, which makes the entity itself originated and therefore not divine.

?
This argument shows that anything caused or contingent cannot be divine. As you examine your own heart, what desires or fears do you instinctively cling to as if they were ultimate? Commit today to surrender one such attachment to Allah alone.

Second Argument: The Distinguishing Attribute Implies Deficiency
Sanusi argues that the distinguishing attribute is either a perfection or a deficiency, both entail divinity is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's logical dilemma that any distinguishing attribute between supposed co-eternal gods necessarily entails deficiency, proving the impossibility of multiple gods.

Core Idea
A distinguishing attribute always implies deficiency — either it's a perfection missing from the other, or it's a deficiency itself.

Sanusi presents a second powerful argument: if there were two gods, they would be distinguished by some accidental attribute. That attribute must be either a perfection or a deficiency. Both options lead to an impossibility: deficiency in one or both gods, which cannot apply to the divine. Let's trace this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Set up the argument
Understand that the argument begins with the premise: if there are two gods, they must be distinguished by an accidental attribute (something not essential). This attribute is the focus of the dilemma.
The attribute is 'accidental' meaning it is not part of the essence of the god.
2
Examine the first horn: attribute is a perfection
If the distinguishing attribute is a perfection (like power or knowledge), then the other god lacks it. Lacking a perfection is a deficiency, so each god would be deficient in essence.
This would mean each god is imperfect, which contradicts divinity.
3
Examine the second horn: attribute is a deficiency
If the distinguishing attribute is a deficiency (like weakness), then that god is described with deficiency from the start, which is clearly impossible.
Both options lead to the same conclusion: the existence of two gods is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً ذلكِ العَرَضى : إما أن يكون كمالاً فقد فات الآخر ، وفوتُ الكمال نقص ؛ فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما ناقصاً في ذاته ؛ وهو وإنْ كانَ ذلكَ العَرَضِيُّ نَقْصاً .. لزمَ أيضاً اتصافُ الإله بالنقص مِنْ أولِ مَرَّةٍ ، وهو ظاهر الاستحالة .
And also that accidental attribute: either it is a perfection (kmalan) — then the other lacks it, and the lack of perfection is a deficiency, so it follows that each of them is deficient in its essence; or if that accidental attribute is a deficiency, it would also necessitate describing God with deficiency from the very beginning, and that is clearly impossible.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against two co-eternal gods. The distinguishing accidental attribute between them is either a perfection or a deficiency. If it is a perfection, then the other god lacks that perfection, and lacking perfection is a deficiency, so each god would be deficient in essence. If the attribute is a deficiency, then God is described with deficiency from the start. Both possibilities are impossible because God must be perfect and free from deficiency.

Key Terms
كمال
نقص
العَرَضى
اتصاف الإله بالنقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the distinguishing attribute could be a perfection that both gods share, so no deficiency arises.
Correction: The attribute is distinctive (العَرَضى) — it distinguishes the two, so it cannot be shared. If it were shared, they would not be distinct.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities for the distinguishing attribute in this argument?
A) It is either necessary or contingent.
B) It is either a perfection or a deficiency.
C) It is either eternal or originated.
D) It is either essential or accidental.

Key Takeaway
The distinguishing attribute between any two supposedly co-eternal gods inevitably leads to deficiency, proving that only one perfect God can exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that any distinction between co-eternal beings leads to deficiency. In your life, when you elevate a person, possession, or ambition to a place of ultimate importance, are you not making it a 'partner' that implies a deficiency in your understanding of God's sufficiency? What must you demote today?
?
If every accidental attribute in a supposed 'god' reveals a flaw, consider the accidental attributes you cling to for identity—your job, your reputation, your abilities. Are you attributing to yourself a 'perfection' that actually reveals your need for God? How does this passage call you to strip away these pretenses and rest in God alone?

Third Argument: Finite or Infinite Number of Gods Impossible
Sanusi presents a logical dilemma against multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the number of gods cannot be finite or infinite, thereby establishing the necessity of tawhid.

Core Idea
A finite number of gods needs a cause; an infinite number is impossible.

This lesson presents Sanusi's third argument for the impossibility of multiple gods. He uses a simple yet powerful dilemma: the number of gods is either finite or infinite. Both options lead to logical contradictions, leaving only the possibility of a single, necessary God.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Notice the structure: 'either...or...'.
The passage is concise but packed with logical reasoning.
2
Analyze the two horns of the dilemma
Identify the two possibilities: finite number and infinite number. Understand why each leads to impossibility.
Focus on the terms 'specifier' (مُخصص) and 'originated' (حادث).
3
Connect to the conclusion
Understand that the argument proves that only one God can exist, as multiple gods are logically impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً تعدُّدُ الإله : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ بعددٍ خاص مُتناه ، فيلزم افتقاره إلى مُخصص ، فيكون حادثاً ، وإما أن يكونَ بعددٍ لا نهاية له ، فيلزم دخولُ ما لا نهاية له في الوجود ، وهو ظاهر الاستحالة .
And also, the multiplicity of God: either it is by a particular finite number, then it necessitates being in need of a specifier (mukhassis), so it would be originated; or it is by an infinite number, then it necessitates the entrance of the infinite into existence, and that is clearly impossible.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument against multiple gods. The number of gods is either finite or infinite. If finite, that specific number would need a cause to specify it (a specifier), making the number contingent and thus the gods originated. If infinite, that would require an actual infinite number of existing entities, which is impossible (as it leads to logical contradictions). Therefore, multiple gods cannot exist.

Key Terms

مُخصص
حادث
ما لا نهاية له
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think an infinite number of gods could exist.
Correction: The author asserts that an actual infinite in existence is 'clearly impossible' due to logical contradictions.

Checkpoint

A) It is possible because God can choose any number.
B) It requires a specifier, implying the gods are originated.
C) It is the only logical possibility.
D) It leads to an actual infinite.

Key Takeaway
The number of gods cannot be finite (requires a cause) or infinite (actual infinite impossible), therefore there must be one God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Fourth Argument: Each God Would Be Incapable of Creating or Destroying
Sanusi argues that each supposed god would be as incapable as other originated beings, contradicting divine omnipotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why multiple gods would logically be incapable of creation and destruction, and how this argument supports tawheed.

Core Idea
If multiple gods existed, each would be as powerless as any creature.

This lesson presents a concise yet powerful argument from Imam al-Sanusi against the possibility of multiple gods. By examining the logical consequences of assuming more than one deity, Sanusi shows that each would be reduced to the same impotence as any created being. This strengthens the Islamic affirmation of God's absolute unity and omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how compact the argument is—one sentence contains both conclusion and proof.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on the terms 'إمكان' (possibility) and 'حدوث' (origination). These are the properties shared by all contingent things. The argument hinges on equality in these properties.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does equality in possibility and origination force each god to be incapable? Consider what it means for something to be originated—it cannot bring itself into existence, let alone create or destroy bodies.
This connects to the broader theme of tawheed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : يلزم أن يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما عاجزاً عن كل ممكن ؛ لمساواتهما في الإمكان والحدوثِ لسائر الحوادثِ التي قد عُرِفَ بالضرورة عجزها عن إيجاد الأجرام وإعدامها .
And also: it follows that each one of them is incapable of every possible thing, because of their equality with other originated things in possibility and origination, which are known by necessity to be incapable of originating or annihilating bodies.

This passage argues that if there were multiple gods, each would be just like any other originated being in terms of possibility and origination. Since originated beings are known to be incapable of creating or destroying physical bodies, each god would also be incapable. This contradicts the idea of God being omnipotent.

Fifth Argument: The Proof of Mutual Hindrance (Dalil al-Tamānu')
Sanusi presents the proof of mutual hindrance against multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof of mutual hindrance and why it demonstrates the impossibility of more than one god.

Core Idea
Even two agreeing gods would inevitably conflict in power and will.

This passage presents a key proof in Islamic theology for the oneness of God: the argument of mutual hindrance (dalil al-tamānu'). It shows that the existence of two gods leads to logical absurdities whether they agree or disagree. The argument relies on the nature of will, power, and the indivisibility of existence. Let's examine it closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to get a clear sense of the argument.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: if the two gods disagree on a possible thing, and if they agree. Notice how each is treated differently.
Disagreement is said to be 'obvious', while agreement requires a deeper logical analysis.
3
Understand why agreement leads to impossibility
Focus on the reasoning for the agreement case: it leads to either division of what is indivisible or acquisition of what is already obtained, both impossible. Conclude that one god must be incapacitated.
This is the heart of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزم أيضاً : عجز المثلين في الألوهية من جهة التمانع بينَ إرادتيهما وقدرتيهما ، سواء اتفقا على ممكن واحدٍ أو اختلفا ، أمَّا إن اختلفا فظاهر . وأما إن اتفقا : فلأنَّ لكلّ ممكن وجوداً واحداً ، فيستحيل أن تَنفُذَ فيه إرادتان وقدرتانِ ، وإلا لزم انقسامُ ما لا ينقسم ، أو تحصيل الحاصل ، فلا بدَّ إذاً مِنْ عجز إحدى القدرتين وإحدى الإرادتين .
And it also follows: the incapacity of the two similars in divinity from the aspect of mutual hindrance (al-tamānu') between their wills and powers, whether they agree on one possible thing or disagree. As for if they disagree, it is obvious. And as for if they agree: because every possible thing has a single existence, so it is impossible for two wills and two powers to execute it; otherwise it would necessitate the division of what is indivisible or the acquisition of what is already obtained. Therefore, inevitably, one of the two powers and one of the two wills is incapacitated.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known proof of mutual hindrance (dalil al-tamānu'). It argues that if there were two gods, their wills and powers would conflict. If they disagree on what to do, the one who cannot achieve his will is weak, contradicting divinity. If they agree on a single action, that action can only have one cause; if both tried to effect it, it would either require dividing an indivisible action or achieving something already achieved, both impossible. Thus, one god would be incapacitated. Therefore, multiple gods are impossible.

Key Terms
التمانع
الإرادتان
القدرتان
انقسام ما لا ينقسم
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if the gods agree on everything, there would be no conflict and multiple gods could exist peacefully.
Correction: The author shows that even if they agree, the nature of a single existent prevents two separate wills and powers from both being effective, leading to impossibility. Agreement does not resolve the logical contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the argument of mutual hindrance, what must happen if two gods agree on a single possible thing?
A) They both effect it perfectly.
B) One of their wills and powers must be incapacitated.
C) The thing comes into existence twice.
D) They share the action harmoniously.

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance shows that multiple gods are impossible because their wills and powers inevitably conflict, leading to incapacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you subtly act as if there are other 'powers' that can affect your destiny, independent of Allah? How does this proof of mutual hindrance challenge that mindset?
?
When you are in a difficult situation and you find yourself relying on your own strength or the help of others, do you ever stop to realize that any true power belongs only to Allah? How can this argument strengthen your reliance on Him alone?
?
If you truly internalize that only One God has absolute will and power, how would it change the way you pray, fear, and hope? What is one concrete step you can take today to align your heart with this truth?

The Perfection Argument Against Multiple Gods
Sanusi presents the perfection-deficiency argument against multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that multiple gods would each be deficient because they would lack perfections possessed by others.

Core Idea
Multiple gods cannot coexist because each would be deficient.

This lesson presents the first of Sanusi's two arguments against polytheism. He assumes that if there were two gods, they would differ in accidental attributes. But any accident that is a perfection in one would be absent in the other, making that other deficient. Since deficiency is impossible for God, multiple gods cannot exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Note the reference to the earlier proof of God's pre-eternality.
2
Understand the premise
Sanusi builds on the idea that any accidental attribute (عَرَضى) in a god is either a perfection or a deficiency. Consider what each option implies for the other god.
The argument works even if the accident is a deficiency from the start.
3
Trace the logical chain
If one god has a perfection the other lacks, that other is deficient. But God cannot be deficient. Therefore, multiple gods are impossible.
This argument refutes the idea of multiple perfect beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحدوث ينافي الألوهية ؛ لِمَا عرفت في برهان قدم الإله وبقائه . وأيضاً ذلكِ العَرَضى : إما أن يكون كمالاً فقد فات الآخر ، وفوتُ الكمال نقص ؛ فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما ناقصاً في ذاته ؛ وهو وإنْ كانَ ذلكَ العَرَضِيُّ نَقْصاً .. لزمَ أيضاً اتصافُ الإله بالنقص مِنْ أولِ مَرَّةٍ ، وهو ظاهر الاستحالة . محال وأيضاً تعدُّدُ الإله : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ بعددٍ خاص مُتناه ، فيلزم افتقاره إلى مُخصص ، فيكون حادثاً ، وإما أن يكونَ بعددٍ لا نهاية له ، فيلزم دخولُ ما لا نهاية له في الوجود ، وهو ظاهر الاستحالة
And contingency is incompatible with divinity, because of what you know from the proof of the pre-eternality and everlastingness of God. Also that accident: either it is a perfection, then the other has missed it, and missing perfection is deficiency; so it would follow that each of them is deficient in its essence; and even if that accident is a deficiency, then the description of God with deficiency would also follow from the first time, and that is clearly impossible. Impossible. And also the multiplicity of gods: either it is a specific finite number, then it would require a specifier, so it would be contingent, or it is an infinite number, then the entrance of the infinite into existence would follow, and that is clearly impossible.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against the possibility of multiple gods based on contingency. The first argument: if there were two gods, each would lack any perfection present in the other, implying deficiency in each, which is impossible for a god. The second argument: the number of gods must be either finite or infinite. If finite, the specific number would require a cause to specify it, making the gods contingent. If infinite, the actual existence of an infinite number is impossible. Both lead to the conclusion that multiple gods cannot exist.

Key Terms
الحدوث (contingency)
الألوهية (divinity)
الآخر (the other)
الكمال (perfection)
النقص (deficiency)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple gods could coexist if each is perfect in a different way, lacking nothing they need.
Correction: Any perfection present in one god is absent in the other, making that other deficient. Deficiency is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, if there were two gods and one possessed a perfection not present in the other, what would follow?
A) Both would still be perfect
B) The one lacking would be deficient, which is impossible for a god
C) The perfection would be shared equally
D) The other god would be non-existent

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's perfection argument shows that multiple gods cannot exist because they would necessarily be deficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly internalised that any deficiency in your concept of God invalidates your worship, would you dare to let a single doubt about His perfection linger in your heart? What one doubt will you actively seek to remove starting today?
?
Sanusi argues that even the possibility of deficiency in a god is impossible. Have you ever treated God as if He were capable of failure, neglect, or injustice in your life? Write down one specific time you blamed God for something—then repent for that implication and reaffirm His absolute perfection in your own words.
?
The argument exposes that polytheism logically collapses into absurdity. But what about the subtle 'gods' of your own soul—your pride, your comfort, your relationships—that you act as though are independent of Allah? Which of these are you willing to dethrone today, and what step will you take to make this real?

The Finite/Infinite Number Argument Against Multiple Gods
Sanusi presents the number argument: finite or infinite, both impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the number of gods must be either finite or infinite, and both options lead to impossibility.

Core Idea
The very number of gods would require a cause, making them contingent.

Sanusi now considers the second impossibility: the number of gods itself. If the number is finite, it needs a specifier, implying contingency. If infinite, actual infinity is impossible. Either way, multiple gods cannot exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the clear dichotomy: finite or infinite number.
This argument does not depend on the first; it stands alone.
2
Analyze the finite branch
If the number of gods is finite, that specific number needs a reason (specifier). Therefore the gods would be contingent, contradicting divinity.
The specifier (mukhassis) is a cause that determines the particular number.
3
Analyze the infinite branch
If the number is infinite, then an actual infinite would exist in reality, which is impossible (according to kalam).
This argument rejects actual infinite sets in existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Impossible. And also the multiplicity of gods: either it is a specific finite number, then it would require a specifier, so it would be contingent, or it is an infinite number, then the entrance of the infinite into existence would follow, and that is clearly impossible.

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against the possibility of multiple gods based on contingency. The first argument: if there were two gods, each would lack any perfection present in the other, implying deficiency in each, which is impossible for a god. The second argument: the number of gods must be either finite or infinite. If finite, the specific number would require a cause to specify it, making the gods contingent.
