Equality to Contingents and Mutual Impediment
Sanusi argues that two equal gods would be incapable like contingents and that their wills would mutually impede.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi argues that two equal gods would each be incapable by comparing them to contingent beings and by showing mutual impediment when they agree or differ on a single act.

Core Idea
Two equal gods logically entail mutual incapability — a single effect cannot serve two independent wills.

Sanusi now presents a second argument for God's oneness, building on the contingency argument. He shows that if there were two gods, they would be no different from contingent beings in their inability to create, and furthermore, their wills would inevitably conflict. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in without analysis.
Notice the two main parts: first about equality to contingents, then about mutual impediment.
2
Identify the two sub-arguments
The passage presents two linked arguments. First, equality to contingents makes each god incapable. Second, mutual impediment (tamānuʻ) when two wills target the same act.
Pay attention to the logical structure: agreement leads to impossibility, disagreement is obvious.
3
Understand the logical contradiction
For a single possible act, only one existence exists. Two wills acting on it either divide the indivisible or produce the already obtained, so one will must be incapable.
This is not a physical but a logical impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : يلزم أن يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما عاجزاً عن كل ممكن ؛ لمساواتهما في الإمكان والحدوثِ لسائر الحوادثِ التي قد عُرِفَ بالضرورة عجزها عن إيجاد الأجرام وإعدامها . ويلزم أيضاً : عجز المثلين في الألوهية من جهة التمانع بينَ إرادتيهما وقدرتيهما ، سواء اتفقا على ممكن واحدٍ أو اختلفا ، أمَّا إن اختلفا فظاهر . وأما إن اتفقا : فلأنَّ لكلّ ممكن وجوداً واحداً ، فيستحيل أن تَنفُذَ فيه إرادتان وقدرتانِ ، وإلا لزم انقسامُ ما لا ينقسم ، أو تحصيل الحاصل ، فلا بدَّ إذاً مِنْ عجز إحدى القدرتين وإحدى الإرادتين ، ويلزم منه عجز الأخرى ؛ لِمَا انعقد بينهما مِنَ المماثلة . هذا كله في المِثْلِ الحقيقي العام . وأَمَّا إذا فُرِضَ المِثْلُ خاصّاً في بعض الصفاتِ ؛ كالقدرة والإرادة مثلاً : فإنَّه يلزمُ الحدوث أيضاً لكلِّ مِنَ المثلين ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدةٍ مِنَ الصفتين المتماثلتين تحتاجُ إلى مُخصص يخصصها بالمحل الذي وُجِدَتْ فيهِ ؛ لقبولِ كلَّ واحدة منهما حينئذ المحلَّينِ ، فيلزم أن تكونَ كل واحدة منهما جائزة الوجودِ حادثةً عارضةً لكلِّ مِنَ الموصوفين وكلُّ واحدٍ منهما لا يمكن أن يعرى عن هذه الصفة الحادثة أو ضدها ، ولا يكون ذلك الضدُّ إلا حادثاً ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ كلٌّ مِنَ حادثاً ، وذلكَ ينافي ما ثبت للإله مِنْ وجوب الوجودِ ، ويلزم حينئذ العجز أيضاً ؛ لأجل الحدوثِ والتمانع إنْ فُرِضَ المِثلُ في القدرة والإرادة
And also: it follows that each of them is incapable of every possible thing, because they are equal in possibility and contingency to other contingents which are known by necessity to be incapable of bringing about or annihilating bodies. And also it follows: the incapability of two equals in divinity from the aspect of mutual impediment between their wills and powers, whether they agree on one possible or differ. As for if they differ, it is obvious. And as for if they agree: because every possible has one existence, so it is impossible for two wills and two powers to execute it, otherwise division of what is indivisible or obtaining the already obtained would follow, so necessarily then there is incapability of one of the powers and one of the wills, and follows from it the incapability of the other because of the likeness between them. All this in the general real likeness. And as for if the likeness is assumed specifically in some attributes, like power and will for example: then contingency also follows for each of the two likes, because each of the two like attributes needs a specifier to specify it to the subject in which it exists, because each of them then accepts both subjects; so it follows that each of them is possible of existence, contingent, accidental to each of the described, and each of them cannot be free from this contingent attribute or its opposite, and that opposite is nothing but contingent, so it follows that each of them is contingent, and that contradicts what is established for God of necessary existence, and then incapability also follows because of contingency and mutual impediment if the likeness is assumed in power and will.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage contains multiple proofs against multiple gods, focusing on mutual impediment (tamānuʻ). First, it argues that if there were two gods, each would be incapable of every possible act because they would be equal to contingent beings known to be incapable of creating bodies. Second, it presents the classic argument of mutual impediment: if two gods agreed on an act, their wills and powers would conflict, leading to either the division of an indivisible effect or a redundant effect, so one must be incapable. If they disagreed, the conflict is obvious. The argument then extends to a specific likeness in attributes like power and will: such attributes would require a specifier, making each god contingent. Thus, no plurality is possible.

Key Terms
العجز (incapability)
التمانع (mutual impediment)
المساواة (equality)
الإرادة (will)
القدرة (power)
المثل (like/equal)
الحدوث (contingency)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutual impediment argument is about physical conflict between two gods.
Correction: The argument is about logical impossibility: a single effect cannot originate from two equal causes without contradiction.

Checkpoint
What does the argument of mutual impediment (tamānuʻ) establish when two gods agree on the same possible act?
A) Both wills can coexist without conflict
B) One of the two powers must be incapable
C) The act is divided between them
D) The act becomes necessary

Key Takeaway
The mutual impediment argument shows that multiple gods logically cannot coexist because a single effect cannot originate from two equal causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a single existence cannot serve two wills, what does that say about your own life when you try to serve both Allah and your desires? Can you honestly claim to have a unified will? What one intention do you need to purify today?
?
Sanusi argues that even if two gods agreed, their partnership would lead to absurdity. Have you ever assumed you could partner your own efforts with Allah's grace without submitting entirely? Where do you need to surrender control and trust that Allah alone is sufficient?
?
The proof from equality to contingents shows that anything equal to a contingent shares its incapability. Do you ever treat created things as if they have independent power alongside Allah? Name one thing you fear or rely on that you must now reframe as utterly dependent on Allah.

Specific Likeness in Attributes Leads to Contingency
Sanusi argues that even partial likeness in attributes leads to contingency and mutual impediment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi extends the argument to show that even if the likeness is limited to specific attributes like power and will, each god would still be contingent and thus incapable.

Core Idea
Even a partial likeness in attributes forces each god to be contingent, contradicting necessary existence.

Sanusi now considers a hypothetical scenario where the two gods are not fully equal but share only certain attributes, such as power and will. He demonstrates that even this limited likeness leads to contingency and mutual impediment, reinforcing the impossibility of multiple gods.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on specific likeness
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the shift from general to specific likeness.
This part builds on the previous argument but now focuses on partial similarity.
2
Understand the need for a specifier
Each shared attribute (e.g., power) could inhere in either god, so it needs a specifier (mukhassis) to determine its subject, indicating it is contingent.
Contingent attributes require an external cause to specify them.
3
See how this leads to contingency of the god
If a god possesses a contingent attribute, and cannot be free of it or its opposite (also contingent), then the god himself becomes contingent, contradicting necessary existence.
Thus even partial likeness undermines divinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : يلزم أن يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما عاجزاً عن كل ممكن ؛ لمساواتهما في الإمكان والحدوثِ لسائر الحوادثِ التي قد عُرِفَ بالضرورة عجزها عن إيجاد الأجرام وإعدامها . ويلزم أيضاً : عجز المثلين في الألوهية من جهة التمانع بينَ إرادتيهما وقدرتيهما ، سواء اتفقا على ممكن واحدٍ أو اختلفا ، أمَّا إن اختلفا فظاهر . وأما إن اتفقا : فلأنَّ لكلّ ممكن وجوداً واحداً ، فيستحيل أن تَنفُذَ فيه إرادتان وقدرتانِ ، وإلا لزم انقسامُ ما لا ينقسم ، أو تحصيل الحاصل ، فلا بدَّ إذاً مِنْ عجز إحدى القدرتين وإحدى الإرادتين ، ويلزم منه عجز الأخرى ؛ لِمَا انعقد بينهما مِنَ المماثلة . هذا كله في المِثْلِ الحقيقي العام . وأَمَّا إذا فُرِضَ المِثْلُ خاصّاً في بعض الصفاتِ ؛ كالقدرة والإرادة مثلاً : فإنَّه يلزمُ الحدوث أيضاً لكلِّ مِنَ المثلين ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدةٍ مِنَ الصفتين المتماثلتين تحتاجُ إلى مُخصص يخصصها بالمحل الذي وُجِدَتْ فيهِ ؛ لقبولِ كلَّ واحدة منهما حينئذ المحلَّينِ ، فيلزم أن تكونَ كل واحدة منهما جائزة الوجودِ حادثةً عارضةً لكلِّ مِنَ الموصوفين وكلُّ واحدٍ منهما لا يمكن أن يعرى عن هذه الصفة الحادثة أو ضدها ، ولا يكون ذلك الضدُّ إلا حادثاً ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ كلٌّ مِنَ حادثاً ، وذلكَ ينافي ما ثبت للإله مِنْ وجوب الوجودِ ، ويلزم حينئذ العجز أيضاً ؛ لأجل الحدوثِ والتمانع إنْ فُرِضَ المِثلُ في القدرة والإرادة
And also: it follows that each of them is incapable of every possible thing, because they are equal in possibility and contingency to other contingents which are known by necessity to be incapable of bringing about or annihilating bodies. And also it follows: the incapability of two equals in divinity from the aspect of mutual impediment between their wills and powers, whether they agree on one possible or differ. As for if they differ, it is obvious. And as for if they agree: because every possible has one existence, so it is impossible for two wills and two powers to execute it, otherwise division of what is indivisible or obtaining the already obtained would follow, so necessarily then there is incapability of one of the powers and one of the wills, and follows from it the incapability of the other because of the likeness between them. All this in the general real likeness. And as for if the likeness is assumed specifically in some attributes, like power and will for example: then contingency also follows for each of the two likes, because each of the two like attributes needs a specifier to specify it to the subject in which it exists, because each of them then accepts both subjects; so it follows that each of them is possible of existence, contingent, accidental to each of the described, and each of them cannot be free from this contingent attribute or its opposite, and that opposite is nothing but contingent, so it follows that each of them is contingent, and that contradicts what is established for God of necessary existence, and then incapability also follows because of contingency and mutual impediment if the likeness is assumed in power and will.

According to Sanusi, why does even a specific likeness in attributes like power lead to contingency?
A) Because each attribute must have a specifier for its subject

Even a limited similarity in attributes forces each supposed god to be contingent, negating necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that even a single shared attribute would make a being contingent. Reflect: In your daily life, do you ever unconsciously attribute any of Allah's qualities—like power, knowledge, or will—to created beings in your mind? How might this logical argument help you root out subtle forms of shirk in your thinking? Commit to catching yourself when you attribute absolute qualities to anyone other than Allah.
?
If every attribute you have (intelligence, strength, wealth) needs a specific determination from Allah to exist in you, what does that tell you about your utter dependence on Him? Consider one area where you feel self-sufficient. How can you actively remind yourself of your contingency and Allah's necessity throughout your day? Write a specific reminder and place it where you will see it.

Allah's Existence

Ibrahim's Argument: The Power of Setting over Rising
Sanusi interprets Ibrahim's statement as an interrogative or citation and explains the use of setting over rising.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets the Quranic verse about Prophet Ibrahim and why he holds that setting provides a clearer proof of contingency than rising.

Core Idea
Setting reveals contingency more clearly than rising because it involves evident decrease.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a key Quranic verse often misunderstood. Prophet Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' is not a literal belief but either a rhetorical question or a citation of his people's claim. Moreover, Sanusi explains why Ibrahim chose the setting of the celestial bodies over their rising as the decisive argument for their contingency. The rising, with its dazzling beauty, can confuse the mind, whereas the setting's clear change makes the argument accessible to all.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the two interpretations of Ibrahim's statement
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi explains that the phrase 'This is my Lord' can be understood as an omitted interrogative (Is this my Lord?) or as a citation of the opponent's claim to refute it.
This clarifies that Ibrahim never actually believed a celestial body was his Lord.
2
Understand why setting is a stronger proof than rising
Notice that Sanusi states both rising and setting indicate contingency, but setting is clearer because it involves change through decrease, which is unmistakable. Rising, though also a proof, has accompanying beauty that can mislead the foolish.
Sanusi emphasizes that the setting's evidence is obvious to both intelligent and foolish.
3
Reflect on the psychological dimension
Consider how Sanusi describes the rising as intoxicating the mind of the foolish, lustful person, preventing reflection. This shows a deep understanding of human psychology in argumentation.
The argument is not just logical but also takes into account human weaknesses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمعنى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : هَذَا رَبِّي ﴾ [الأنعام : ٧٦] : أهذا ربي ؟! على ما تزعمون ، بحذفِ حرفِ الاستفهام ، أو مِنْ بابِ ذكرِ دعوى الخصم لإقامة البرهان على إبطالها وطلوع هذه الكواكب بعد أن لم تكن هو في الاستدلال به على حدوثها كالأفول ، إلا أنَّهُ عليهِ الصلاة والسلامُ إِنَّما أَخَّرَ الاستدلال على حدوثها إلى رؤيةِ أُفولِها لِمَا في الأفولِ مِنَ التغيرِ بالنقصانِ ، فدلالته على حدوثِ تلك الكواكب وعدم صلاحيتها للربوبية واضح للذكي والغبي ، أمَّا طلوعها - وإنْ كانَ دليلاً على حدوثها مِنْ ناحية تجدُّدِهِ بعدَ أنْ لم يكن - فلأنَّهُ لمَّا كانَ فيهِ كمال لها لِمَا صاحبَهُ مِنْ تلكَ الأنوار التي تُوجَدُ لذاتِ النفس والامتدادِ إليها بالأبصار .. قد تُسكِرُ عقل الغبي الشهواني المقلَّدِ أوِ المعاندِ ، فلا يَتأَمَّلُ في وجهِ دلالتِهِ على الحدوثِ ، ولا يصغي لسماعها
The meaning of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'This is my Lord' [al-An'am 76]: (Is this my Lord?! as you claim?) by omitting the interrogative particle, or it is from the category of mentioning the opponent's claim in order to establish the proof for its invalidation. And the rising of these celestial bodies after they were not is in inference upon their contingency like the setting, except that he, peace and blessings be upon him, delayed the inference upon their contingency until seeing their setting because in the setting there is change through decrease, so its indication of the contingency of those celestial bodies and their unsuitability for lordship is clear to both the intelligent and the foolish. As for their rising -- although it is a proof of their contingency from the aspect of their renewal after not being -- because since it contains perfection for them due to what accompanies it of those lights that are found for the pleasure of the soul and the extension of glances toward them, it may intoxicate the mind of the foolish, lustful, blind follower or obstinate person, so he does not reflect on the face of its indication of contingency, nor does he listen to it.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of Prophet Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' in the Qur'an. The author provides two possible interpretations: either it is an interrogative with the question mark omitted, meaning 'Is this my Lord?' as a challenge to his people's claim, or it is a citation of the opponent's claim in order to refute it. Then the author discusses why Ibrahim used the setting (ufūl) of the celestial bodies rather than their rising (ṭulūʿ) as an argument for their contingency. While both rising and setting indicate contingency, rising involves apparent beauty and increase that can dazzle the mind of a foolish, lustful, or obstinate person, preventing them from reflecting on the argument. Therefore, the setting is a clearer proof because it involves change through decrease, making the argument for contingency evident to everyone.

Key Terms
الاستفهام
الأفول
الطلوع
الدلالة
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Ibrahim actually believed a celestial body was his Lord.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that Ibrahim's statement was either a rhetorical question or a citation of his people's claim to refute it, not a literal belief.

Checkpoint
Why did Prophet Ibrahim use the setting (ufūl) rather than the rising (ṭulūʿ) as an argument for the contingency of celestial bodies?
A) Because setting is more dramatic and compelling.
B) Because rising has apparent beauty that can intoxicate the mind of the foolish and prevent reflection.
C) Because setting is a direct command from Allah.
D) Because rising does not indicate contingency at all.

Key Takeaway
Prophet Ibrahim's use of setting over rising as a proof for God's existence is a masterful example of effective argumentation that takes into account human psychology and the clarity of evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the beauty of a sunrise or a full moon, does it draw you closer to recognizing your need for Allah, or does it distract you like the 'foolish' described in this lesson? What would it take for you to see past the beauty to the contingency?
?
Ibrahim delayed his argument to the moment of setting—when what seemed powerful vanished. Have you ever experienced a 'setting' in your life—a loss, a failure, or an end of something you relied on—that made Allah's lordship undeniable? Did you respond with submission, or did you cling to what was vanishing?
?
The foolish person is 'intoxicated' by what pleases the soul. What pleasure in your life right now (a relationship, a possession, an achievement) might be keeping you from deeply reflecting on your absolute dependence on Allah? Will you deliberately set it aside to see the truth more clearly?
